Second Type of Irtibat: Connection of the Branch to a Mere Cause
Defining the second type of ittisal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a mere cause (sabab mahd) and an effective cause ('illa) and apply the rule that metaphorical usage (isti'ara) is valid only from the cause to the effect in this type of causal connection.

Core Idea
A mere cause allows metaphorical transfer only from cause to effect, not reverse.

In Hanafi usul, not all causal relationships are symmetrical. This lesson introduces the second type of ittisal (causal connection), where the cause is a 'mere cause' (sabab mahd)—something that leads to a ruling but was not specifically placed for it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for valid metaphorical usage (isti'ara) in legal language, particularly when interpreting words used in legal contexts such as manumission and divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of cause
Differentiate between 'illa (effective cause) and sabab mahd (mere cause). Note that 'illa is placed specifically for the ruling, while sabab mahd is not.
This distinction is fundamental to the Hanafi theory of causation.
2
Understand the direction of dependency
Recognize that the effect depends on the cause, but the cause is independent of the effect. This determines the valid direction of isti'ara.
Independence of the cause is key: the cause exists without needing the effect.
3
Analyze the manumission example
The word 'itq is an 'illa for removal of milk ar-raqabah (ownership of corpus) but a sabab mahd for removal of milk al-mut'ah (right of enjoyment). The effect (removal of mut'ah) depends on the cause ('itq).
Here, the cause word 'itq is used for the effect removal of mut'ah.
4
Derive the rule for isti'ara
Conclude that using the cause word to mean the effect is valid, but not the opposite. This has practical consequences: saying 'anti hurr' to one's wife can effect divorce if intended.
This rule applies in fiqh whenever a mere cause is used to refer to its effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالثَّانِي إِتِصالُ الْفَرْعِ بِمَا هُوَ سَبَبٌ مَحْضٌ لَيْسَ بِعِلَّةٍ وُضِعَتْ لَهُ كَاتِّصَالِ زَوَالِ مِلْكِ الْمُتْعَةِ بِالْفَاظِ الْعِتْقِ تَبَعًا لِزَوَالِ مِلْكِ الرَّقْبَةِ وَإِنَّهُ يُوجِبُ اِسْتِعَارَةَ الْأَصْلِ لِلْفَرْعِ وَالسَّبَبَ لِلْحُكْمِ دُونَ عَكْسِهِ لِأَنَّ اِتِّصَالَ الْفَرْعِ بِالْأَصْلِ فِي حَقِّ الْأَصْلِ فِي حُكْمِ الْعَدَمِ لِاسْتِغْنَائِهِ عَنِ الْفَرْعِ. ترجمہ : اتصال سبب کی دوسری قسم فرع (حکم، مسبب) کا اتصال ہے اس چیز کے ساتھ جو سبب محض ہے ایسی علت نہیں ہے جو فرع کے لئے وضع کی گئی ہو، جیسے ملک متعہ کے زوال کا اتصال عتق کے الفاظ سے ملک رقبہ کے زوال کے تابع ہو کر، اور یہ دوسری قسم ثابت کرتی ہے اصل کے استعارہ کو فرع کے لئے اور سبب کے استعارہ کو حکم کے لئے، نہ کہ اس کے برعکس، اس لئے کہ فرع کا اتصال اصل کے ساتھ اصل مراد لینے کے حق میں معدوم کے حکم میں ہے اصل اس سے مستغنی ہونے کی وجہ سے فرع سے۔ تشریح: مصنف اتصال سبب کی دوسری قسم بیان کر رہے ہیں، جس میں سبب ومسبب کے درمیان اتصال ہوتا ہے، سبب کے ساتھ محض کی قید لگا کر علت کو خارج کردیا، کیونکہ کبھی سبب سے علت مراد ہوتی ہے، تو محض کہہ کر علت کو خارج کر دیا پھر صاف لفظوں میں بھی علت کی نفی کر دی، چنانچہ کہا لیس بعلة وضعت له - کہ وہ ایسی علت نہ ہو جو حکم کے لئے وضع کی گئی ہو، بلکہ یہاں سبب محض مراد ہے۔ بہر حال دوسری قسم فرع ( جس کو حکم اور مسبب بھی کہتے ہیں ) کا اتصال سبب محض (جس کو اصل بھی کہتے ہیں) کے ساتھ ہو، سبب اور علت میں فرق ہے، علت حکم کے لئے وضع ہوتی ہے اور حکم علت کی طرف وجوداً ووجوباً منسوب ہوتا ہے، اور سبب صرف مفضی الی الحکم ہوتا ہے، اب سبب و مسبب کے درمیان اتصال کو مثال سے سمجھو، کسی نے اپنی باندی سے کہا انت حرة یا اعتقتک، تو ان الفاظ عتق سے، پہلے اس باندی کا ملک رقبہ زائل ہو گا اور ملک رقبہ کے واسطہ سے ملک متعہ زائل ہوگا۔ تو عتق کا لفظ سبب ہے، ملک متعہ کے زوال کے لئے اور ملک متعہ کا زوال مسبب ہے، کیونکہ عتق کا لفظ ملک متعہ کے زوال کی طرف مفضی ہے، ملک متعہ کے زوال کے لئے یہ لفظ وضع نہیں ہوا ہے وضع تو ہوا ہے ملک رقبہ کے زوال کے لئے، لہذا عتق ملک رقبہ کے زوال کے لئے تو علت بنے گا، البتہ عتق کے لفظ نے ملک رقبہ کے زوال کے واسطہ سے ملک متعہ کے زوال تک پہنچا دیا، اس لئے عتق کو ملک متعہ کے زوال کے لئے سبب کہیں گے، اور چونکہ سبب ہے علت نہیں، اس لئے ضروری نہیں ہے کہ عتق ملک متعہ کے زوال تک ہمیشہ پہنچائے کبھی پہنچائے گا کبھی نہیں جیسے اپنی باندی سے کہا تو عتق ملک متعہ کے زوال تک پہنچائے گا اور غلام سے کہا تو نہیں پہنچائے گا، البتہ عتق کا لفظ ملک رقبہ کے زوال تک یقینی طور پر پہنچائے گا، تو معلوم ہوا کہ عتق ملک رقبہ کے لئے تو علت ہے لیکن ملک متعہ کے زوال کے لئے سبب ہے۔ اور ملک متعہ کا زوال طلاق سے ہوتا ہے تو گویا انت حرة سبب ہوا اور انت طالق مسبب ہوا، لہذا اگر کوئی اپنی بیوی کو انتِ حُر کہے اور انتِ طالق مراد لے تو صحیح ہے کیونکہ سبب بولکر مسبب مراد لے رہا ہے، لہذا بیوی پر طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی لیکن اگر کسی نے اپنی باندی کو انتِ طَالِقٌ کہا اور أَنْتِ حُرَّةً مراد لیا یعنی مسبب بولکر سبب مراد لیا تو صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ سبب ومسبب کے درمیان اتصال والی قسم میں استعارہ صرف ایک طرف سے درست ہے یعنی سبب بولکر مسبب مراد لینا تو صحیح ہے کیونکہ مسبب اپنے ثبوت میں سبب کا محتاج ہے تو مسبب کی طرف سے سبب کے ساتھ اتصال ہے، لیکن مسبب بولکر سبب مراد لینا صحیح نہیں ہے، کیونکہ
And the second (type) is the connection of the branch (far') to that which is a mere cause (sabab mahd), not an effective cause ('illa) placed for it, like the connection of the removal of the right of enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah) to the words of manumission (alfaz al-'itq) as a consequence of the removal of ownership of the corpus (milk al-raqabah). This second type necessitates the metaphorical use (isti'ara) of the original (asl) for the branch (far') and of the cause for the ruling, not the reverse, because the connection of the branch to the original, in respect of the original, is in the status of non-existence due to the original's being independent of the branch. Translation: The second type of cause-connection is the connection of the branch (ruling, effect) to that which is a mere cause, not an effective cause placed for the branch. For example, the connection of the removal of milk al-mut'ah to the words of manumission, following the removal of milk al-raqabah. This second type establishes the metaphorical use of the original for the branch and of the cause for the ruling, not the reverse, because the connection of the branch to the original, in the right of intending the original, is in the status of non-existence due to the original's independence from the branch. Elaboration: The author is explaining the second type of cause-connection, in which there is a connection between cause and effect. By adding the restriction 'mere' to cause, he excludes 'illa, because sometimes 'illa is meant by 'sabab'. So by saying 'mere' he excludes 'illa, then he further negates 'illa explicitly by saying 'laysa bi-'illatin wudi'at lahu' (it is not an effective cause placed for it). Rather, here mere cause is intended. In any case, the second type is the connection of the branch (also called ruling and effect) with the mere cause (also called original). There is a difference between cause and effective cause: an effective cause ('illa) is placed for the ruling, and the ruling is attributed to it existentially and obligatorily (wujudan wa wujuban), while a cause (sabab) is merely that which leads to the ruling (mufdi ila al-hukm). Now understand the connection between cause and effect with an example. Someone said to his slave-girl 'anti hurra' or 'a'taqtuki'. By the words of manumission, first the ownership of the corpus (milk al-raqabah) is removed, and through that, the right of enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah) is removed. So the word 'itq is a cause for the removal of milk al-mut'ah, and the removal of milk al-mut'ah is the effect, because the word 'itq leads to the removal of milk al-mut'ah; it was not placed for the removal of milk al-mut'ah, but rather was placed for the removal of milk al-raqabah. Therefore, 'itq is an effective cause for the removal of milk al-raqabah, but the word 'itq, through the removal of milk al-raqabah, reaches the removal of milk al-mut'ah, so 'itq is called a cause for the removal of milk al-mut'ah. And since it is a cause, not an effective cause, it is not necessary that 'itq always leads to the removal of milk al-mut'ah; sometimes it leads, sometimes not. For example, if said to his slave-girl, 'itq leads to the removal of milk al-mut'ah, but if said to a male slave, it does not. However, the word 'itq definitely leads to removal of milk al-raqabah. So it is clear that 'itq is an effective cause for removal of milk al-raqabah but a cause for removal of milk al-mut'ah. And the removal of milk al-mut'ah occurs through divorce. So 'anti hurra' became the cause and 'anti taliq' became the effect. Therefore, if someone says to his wife 'anti hurr' intending 'anti taliq', it is valid because he is using the cause to mean the effect, so divorce occurs. But if someone says to his slave-girl 'anti taliq' intending 'anti hurra' — i.e., using the effect to mean the cause — it is not valid, because in this type of cause-effect connection, isti'ara is correct only in one direction: using the cause to mean the effect is valid because the effect in its existence is in need of the cause, so from the side of the effect there is a connection to the cause. But using the effect to mean the cause is not valid, because
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of ittisal (connection) between a cause and its effect, specifically where the cause is a 'mere cause' (sabab mahd) and not an effective cause ('illa) placed specifically for that effect. The author illustrates with the example of manumission ('itq). The word 'itq is placed for removing ownership of the corpus (raqabah), but it also leads to removing the right of enjoyment (mut'ah) as a necessary consequence. Thus, 'itq is an effective cause for the former but a mere cause for the latter. Since the effect (removal of mut'ah) depends on the cause, using the cause word ('itq or 'anti hurra) to mean the effect (talaq) is valid as a metaphorical transfer. Conversely, using the effect word (taliq) to mean the cause ('itq) is invalid because the cause is independent of the effect. This principle has practical fiqhi consequences: saying 'anti hurr' to one's wife intends talaq, and it is effective; but saying 'anti taliq' to a slave-girl intending manumission is ineffective. The author emphasizes that this type of ittisal is distinct from the first type (where the cause is an effective cause) and that isti'ara is only valid one way.

Key Terms
سبب محض (sabab mahd): A mere cause that leads to a ruling but was not specifically placed for it; it is not an effective cause.
علة ('illa): An effective cause placed specifically for a ruling; the ruling is attributed to it existentially and obligatorily (wujudan wa wujuban).
مسبب (musabbab): The effect or ruling that results from the cause.
فرع (far'): Branch; here, the effect or ruling that depends on the cause.
اصل (asl): Original; here, the cause (sabab) which is independent of the effect.
اتصال (ittisal): Connection; the logical or causal link between two things that allows metaphorical transference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second type of ittisal, if a man says to his wife 'anti hurr' intending 'anti taliq' (divorce), what is the ruling?
A) Divorce does not occur because he used a word for manumission.
B) Divorce occurs because he used the cause (manumission) to mean the effect (divorce).
C) Divorce occurs only if he explicitly intends 'itq.
D) The statement is invalid because 'hurr' cannot be used for women.

Key Takeaway
In the second type of ittisal, metaphorical transfer is valid only from the mere cause to its effect, never the reverse.

Interpreting Plural 'Mawali' in Hanafi Wills
Interpretation of plural mawali in wills

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that in inheritance and wills, the plural is interpreted as a minimum of two, and explain the resulting share for a single legatee.

Core Idea
In inheritance and wills, plural means two—one legatee gets half the bequest.

This passage from Al-Husami tackles a subtle interplay between linguistic meaning and legal practice. When an Arab makes a will for his 'mawali', the plural form clashes with the limited number of actual intended beneficiaries. The Hanafi school resolves this by applying a special rule: in the context of inheritance and wills, the plural is treated as two. This lesson unpacks that rule and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
An Arab testator bequeaths one-third of his property to his 'mawali'. Since an Arab cannot be a slave, the only possible literal (haqiqi) meaning is his own freed slave (mu'tiq). The metaphorical (majazi) meaning (slaves of slaves) is excluded.
The word mawali is plural, but the testator likely has only one freed slave.
2
Recognize the linguistic ambiguity
The plural form mawali linguistically indicates at least three individuals. Yet the intended recipient is one person. This creates a conflict between general Arabic grammar and the specific fiqh context.
General rule: jam' = minimum three. Fiqh rule: in inheritance/wills, jam' = minimum two.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
The Hanafi school holds that in matters of inheritance and wills, the plural is interpreted as a minimum of two (not three). This is a specific legal rule overriding the general linguistic rule.
The passage states: 'in inheritance and wills, two is given the ruling of plural'.
4
Derive the share
Since the plural is treated as two, the one-third bequest is divided into two shares. One legatee receives half of the one-third, i.e., one-sixth of the estate.
The remaining one-sixth (the other half) goes to the testator's heirs.
5
Justify the rule
The rule is based on Hanafi usul: custom ('urf) and necessity in inheritance distribution require a deviation from the strict linguistic minimum.
No explicit text is cited; the rule is a well-established Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اب عبارت سمجھیں، فرماتے ہیں کہ کسی عربی نے اپنے موالی کے لئے تہائی مال کی وصیت کی ، تو یہاں موالی کے معنی معتق ( آزاد کردہ غلام) ہے، یہاں معتق مراد نہیں ہو سکتا ہے، کیوں کہ عربوں میں غلامی نہیں ہوسکتی ہے، لہذا اس کا کوئی معتق نہیں ہوگا، تو یہاں مولی کا معنی معتق معین ہو گئے ، اب حقیقی معنی اس کا خود کا آزاد کردہ غلام، اور مجازی معنی غلاموں کے غلام ، اور اس کا ایک ہی معتق ہے اور اس کے معتق کے معتق بہت ہیں ، لہذا اس کے حقیقی معنی اس کا خود کا معتق جب مراد لے لیا تو غلاموں کے غلام کے لئے وصیت نہیں ہوگی ، لہذا اس کے معتق کو ثلث کا آدھا دیکر باقی موصی کے وارثوں کو دے دیا جائے گا۔ سوال پیدا ہوتا ہے کہ اس نے موالی جمع کا صیغہ استعمال کیا ہے اور اس کے کم سے کم تین فرد ہیں ، تو اس کو ثلث کا تیسرا حصہ دینا چاہیے نہ کہ آدھا فما الجواب جواب یہ ہے کہ آپ کی بات بالکل صحیح ہے مگر میراث اور وصیت میں دو کو جمع کا حکم دیا جاتا ہے ،لہذا ایک جمع کا نصف شمار ہوگا ، اس لئے اس کو ثلث کا آدھا ملے گا۔
Now understand the text: He says that an Arab made a will of one-third of his property for his mawali. Here mawali means freed slave (mu'tiq). The freed slave cannot be intended here because Arabs cannot be slaves, so he has no freed slave. So here mawali's meaning is determined as freed slave. Now its real (haqiqi) meaning is his own freed slave, and its metaphorical (majazi) meaning is slave of slaves. And he has one freed slave, and his freed slave's freed slaves are many. So when its real meaning is taken as his own freed slave, the will for the slave of slaves is not valid. So his freed slave is given half of the one-third, and the rest is given to the testator's heirs. A question arises: He used the plural form mawali, and its minimum is three individuals, so he should be given one-third of the one-third, not half. So what is the answer? The answer is that your point is absolutely correct, but in inheritance and wills, two is given the ruling of plural. Therefore, one plural is counted as half. So he gets half of the one-third.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific ruling in inheritance and wills within the Hanafi school. The scenario: an Arab (who cannot be a slave) makes a will of one-third of his property for his 'mawali'. The word 'mawali' can be literal (his own freed slave) or metaphorical (slaves of his freed slave). Since the testator has no freed slave (being an Arab), the literal meaning is impossible; thus the intended meaning must be his freed slave (if he had one). But here the text explains that the will is valid only for the literal freed slave, not for the metaphorical ones. A question arises: the word 'mawali' is plural (minimum three in Arabic), so why does the legatee (one person) get half of the one-third instead of one-third? The answer: in inheritance and wills (Hanafi fiqh), the plural is interpreted as a minimum of two. Therefore, one legatee receives half of the one-third (i.e., the share of two). This ruling is an application of the usul principle that in certain contexts (like inheritance), the plural is not taken at its literal minimum of three but is adapted to the specific fiqh rules. The passage demonstrates the interplay between linguistic interpretation (haqiqah vs. majaz) and fiqh rules (plural meaning two in inheritance).

Key Terms
موالی (mawālī): Plural of mawlā; can mean freed slave (mu'tiq) or patron. In this context, it refers to the freed slave of the testator.
جمع (jam‘): Plural form; in Arabic, minimum of three, but in inheritance and wills, ruled as two by Hanafi jurists.
حقیقی (ḥaqīqī): Literal or real meaning of a word, as opposed to metaphorical (majāzī).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the ruling regarding the plural form 'mawali' in a will?
A) The plural always means three or more, so each of three legatees gets a third.
B) The plural is interpreted as two, so one legatee receives half of the bequeathed share.
C) The plural includes all metaphorical meanings, covering slaves of slaves.
D) The plural is void due to ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi inheritance and wills, the plural is taken as two, so a single legatee receives half of the bequeathed share.

Rule of Haqiqah and Majaz in Legal Interpretation
Explanation of the principle that literal meaning takes precedence over metaphorical meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of haqiqah and majaz to identify when metaphorical meaning is used and to derive the correct legal ruling for oaths and agency contracts.

Core Idea
Literal meaning (haqiqah) is primary; metaphorical (majaz) is only used when literal is impossible or abandoned by custom or Shari'ah.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, interpreting words in legal texts begins with the literal meaning. However, when acting literally becomes impossible or the literal sense has been abandoned by custom or Shari'ah, the metaphorical meaning takes over. This rule governs oaths, contracts, and agency appointments, ensuring justice aligns with legal objectives. This lesson unpacks the principle and its two exceptions through concrete examples from oaths and litigation agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Default Rule
State the general Hanafi principle: haqiqah is the original meaning of a word and must be acted upon as long as it is possible. Majaz (metaphorical meaning) is only resorted to when haqiqah cannot be applied.
This mirrors the linguistic principle that the original (asl) takes precedence over the derived (far').
2
Recognize the Two Exceptions
Identify the two circumstances that justify abandoning haqiqah: (1) impossibility (ta'adhdhur) – acting literally is extremely difficult or impossible; (2) abandonment (hijran) – the literal meaning, though possible, is no longer used in custom ('urf) or has been abandoned by Shari'ah.
Abandonment can be customary or legal; both are treated similarly in effect.
3
Apply to Oath Examples
Analyze the date palm oath: literal (eating the tree) is impossible, so majaz (fruit or value) is intended. Then analyze the foot-in-house oath: literal (barefoot entry, walking, or ownership) is abandoned in custom, so majaz (absolute entry) is taken.
These examples show how impossibility and abandonment each trigger the shift to metaphorical meaning.
4
Extend to Agency for Litigation
Examine the wakalah bil khusoomah: literal meaning (agent blindly supports client) is abandoned by Shari'ah because it entails injustice. Therefore, the metaphor (agent gives an absolute answer, admitting or denying regardless of client's interest) is intended.
This demonstrates how Shari'ah-based abandonment (hijran shar'i) operates like customary abandonment.
5
Examine the Child Oath Example
Analyze the oath not to speak to a child: literal meaning (restricted to childhood) is abandoned by Shari'ah (mercy towards children is obligatory). Therefore, majaz applies: the oath covers any age of the child's existence.
The hadith 'He who does not show mercy to our young...' supports the Shari'ah's abandonment of the literal restriction.

مستعار اصل کا مقابل نہیں ہو سکتا ، پس اگر حقیقی معنی متعذر ہوں جیسے جب قسم کھائی کہ اس کھجور کے درخت سے نہیں کھائے گا، یا حقیقی معنی متروک ہوں ( عرف میں ) جیسے جب قسم کھائی کہ اپنا قدم نہیں رکھے گا فلاں کے گھر میں تو کلام کو مجاز کی طرف پھیر دیا جائے گا۔
تشریح:- مصنف اللہ یہاں حقیقت و مجاز کا حکم بیان فرمارہے ہیں کہ حقیقت و مجاز کا حکم یہ ہے کہ جب تک حقیقت پر عمل ممکن ہو، مجاز پر عمل نہیں کیا جائے گا ، اس لئے کہ حقیقت اصل ہے اور مجاز مستعار ہے یعنی عاریت پر لیا ہوا ہے، دوسرے لفظوں میں یوں کہو کہ حقیقت اصل ہے اور مجاز خلیفہ ہے تو جب تک اصل موجود ہے مستعار اور خلیفہ مراد نہیں لیا جاسکتا ہے، کیونکہ مستعار اور خلیفہ اصل کے مقابل نہیں ہوسکتا ہے۔
ہاں اگر حقیقت پر عمل متعد ر ہو یعنی بغیر تکلف اور مشقت کے اس تک نہ پہنچا جا سکے یا حقیقت مہجورہ ہو یعنی اس پر عمل تو ہو سکتا ہو مگر عرف میں یہ معنی چھوڑ دیے گئے ہوں ، تو ان دونوں صورتوں میں کلام سے حقیقی معنی مراد نہیں لیں گے بلکہ کلام کو مجازی معنی کی طرف پھیر دیا جائے گا۔ حقیقت متعذرہ کی مثال جیسے کسی نے قسم کھائی - لا أكل من هذه النخله - اس درخت سے نہیں کھاؤنگا، تو اس کے حقیقی معنی اس درخت کو کھانا ، اور مجازی معنی اس درخت کے پھل کھانا ، اگر درخت پھل دار نہ ہو تو اس کی قیمت کھانا، تو یہاں حقیقی معنی متعذر اور مشکل ہے، لہذا اس کے مجازی معنی مراد ہو نگے یعنی پھل یا قیمت ، لہذا تکلف کر کے اگر اس درخت کو کھائے گا تو حانث نہ ہوگا۔

حقیقت مہجورہ کی مثال جیسے کسی نے قسم کھائی - لا يضع قدمه فی دار فلان - فلاں کے گھر میں وہ قدم نہیں رکھے گا، تو اس کے حقیقی معنی ننگے پیر داخل ہونا، پیدل داخل ہونا، ملکیت کے گھر میں داخل ہونا ہے، اور مجازی معنی مطلق داخل ہونا جیسے پہلے اس کی تفصیل گذر چکی تو یہاں حقیقی معنی عرفاً متروک ہیں، لہذا مجازی معنی مراد ہوں گے۔
وَعَلَى هَذَا قُلْنَا اِنَّ التَّوْكِيْلَ بِالْخُصُومَةِ يَنْصَرِفْ إِلى مُطْلَقِ الْجَوَابِ لِاَنَّ الْحَقِيْقَةَ مَهْجُوْرَةٌ شَرْعًا وَالْمَهْجُورُ شَرْعًا بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْمَهْجُوْرِ عَادَتَا أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ مَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يُكَلِّمُ هَذَا الصَّبِيَّ لَمْ يَتَقَيَّدُ بِزَمَانِ صِبَاهُ لِأَنَّ هِجْرَانَ الصَّبِيِّ مَهْجُورٌ شَرُعًا
ترجمہ :۔ اسی بنا پر ہم نے کہا کہ خصومت کا وکیل بنانا لوٹے گا مطلق جواب کی طرف ، اس لئے کہ حقیقت مہجور ہے شرعاً، اور جو چیز شرعا مجبور ہو وہ عادة مہجور کے درجہ میں ہے، کیا تو نہیں دیکھتا ہے کہ اگر کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ وہ بات نہیں کرے گا اس بچہ سے، تو یہ قسم نہیں مقید ہوگی اس کے بچپن کے زمانے کے ساتھ ، اس لئے کہ بچہ کو چھوڑ نا یعنی اس سے ترک تکلم شرعا مهجور ہے۔ تشریح:- او پر اصول بتایا کہ حقیقت جب مہجور ہو تو مجاز مراد لیا جائے گا، مصنف اس کی دومثالیں ذکر کر رہے ہیں، پہلی مثال: - زید نے خالد پر ایک ہزار کا دعوی کیا تو زید مدعی اور خالد مدعی علیہ ہوا ، اب خالد نے اپنی طرف سے خصومت کے لئے راشد کو وکیل بنایا، تو راشد وکیل بالخصومہ ہوا، اب وکیل بالخصومہ بنے کا حقیقی معنی یہ ہیں کہ وکیل اپنے مؤکل یعنی خالد کی طرف داری کرے حمایت کرے ، چاہے وہ صحیح ہو یا غلط، خالد کی موافقت میں صرف انکار ہی کرتا رہے، اقرار نہ کرے۔ مگر یہ حقیقی معنی شرعاً مہجور ہے کیونکہ شرعاً بے جا طرفداری جائز نہیں، اور جو چیز شرعاً مهجور ہو وہ بمنزلہ عادة مہجور کے ہے، اس لئے کہ مسلمان کی شان سے یہ بعید ہے کہ شرعا جو چیز جائز نہ ہو اس کو اپنی عادت و عمل بنائے بلکہ وہ اس کو چھوڑ دے گا، پس جب شرعاً حقیقی معنی متروک ہیں، تو اس کے مجازی معنی مراد لئے جائیں گے۔ اور توکیل بالخصومت کے مجازی معنی یہ ہیں کہ وکیل مطلق جواب دے ، مدعی کے دعوی پر نعم یالا سے جواب دے، یعنی ہاں کہہ کر مدعی کے دعوی کو ثابت کرے اور اپنے مؤکل پر ہزار روپئے لازم کرے یا لا کہہ کر مدعی کے دعوی کو رد کرے اور اپنے مؤکل پر ہزار روپئے کا انکار کرے، تو مجازی معنی یہ ہوں گے کہ وکیل اپنے مؤکل کی حمایت نہ کرے بلکہ مطلق جواب دے، چاہے اس کے موافقت میں ہو یا اس کے خلاف ، اور یہی معنی یہاں مراد لئے جائیں گے۔
دوسری مثال: کسی نے قسم کھائی لا يكلم هذا الصبی کہ وہ اس بچہ سے بات نہیں کرے گا تو اس کلام کے حقیقی معنی ہے کہ قسم اس کے بچپن کے زمانہ کے ساتھ خاص ہو یعنی اس کے بچہ ہونے کی حالت میں بات نہیں کرونگا مگر یہ معنی شرعا متروک ہے، اس لئے کہ حدیث میں فرمایا من لم يرحم صغیر نا فليس منا ۔ معلوم ہوا کہ بچہ سے ترک تعلق حرام ہے اور شرعاً متروک عادةً متروک کے درجہ میں ہے، لہذا یہاں حقیقی معنی مراد نہیں لیں گے بلکہ مجازی معنی مراد لئے جائیں گے اور مجازی معنی ہیں ، بچہ سے اس کی ذات مراد ہے یعنی اس کی ذات سے بات نہ کرونگا لہذا بچپن میں اس کی جوانی میں ، یا بوڑھاپے میں جب بھی بات کرے گا حانث ہو جائے گا ، کیونکہ ذات ہر وقت موجود ہے، پس یہاں حقیقت مہجور ہونے کی وجہ سے مجاز مرا دلیا گیا۔ مگر یہ اس صورت میں ہے جب کہ اس نے ”هذا الصبی کہا ہو، اگر صبیا کہا تو پھر قسم اس کے بچپن کے
Al-Faidh al-Hijazi vol. 44. Urdu commentary on al-Muntakhab al-Husami. The borrowed (majaz) cannot be equivalent to the original (haqiqah). So if the literal meaning is impossible (mut'adhir), like when one swears: 'I will not eat from this date palm tree,' or the literal meaning is abandoned (mahjoor) in custom, like when one swears: 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house,' then the speech is turned to the metaphorical meaning.

Explanation: The author, may Allah have mercy on him, is here stating the rule of haqiqah and majaz: that as long as acting upon the literal meaning is possible, the metaphorical meaning will not be acted upon, because haqiqah is the original and majaz is borrowed, i.e., taken on loan. In other words, haqiqah is the original and majaz is the deputy; so as long as the original exists, the borrowed and the deputy cannot be intended, because the borrowed and the deputy cannot be equivalent to the original.

Yes, if acting upon the literal meaning is impossible, i.e., it cannot be reached without effort and hardship, or the literal meaning is abandoned (mahjoorah), i.e., it is possible to act upon it but these meanings have been left in custom, then in both cases the literal meaning will not be intended from the speech; rather, the speech will be turned to the metaphorical meaning. Example of impossible literal meaning: someone swore 'La akulu min hadhihi al-nakhlah' (I will not eat from this date palm). The literal meaning is eating the tree itself, and the metaphorical meaning is eating its fruit; if the tree does not bear fruit, then [it means] eating its value. Here the literal meaning is impossible and difficult, so the metaphorical meaning (fruit or value) will be intended. Therefore, if he forces himself to eat the tree itself, he will not be considered an oath-breaker (hanith).

Example of abandoned literal meaning: someone swore 'La yada'u qadamahu fi dari fulan' (He will not place his foot in so-and-so's house). The literal meanings are entering barefoot, entering on foot, entering into the owned house; the metaphorical meaning is absolute entering, as already detailed. Here the literal meaning is customarily abandoned, so the metaphorical meaning will be intended.

'And upon this we said that the agency for litigation (al-tawkeel bi al-khusoomah) turns to absolute answer (mutlaq al-jawab), because the literal meaning is abandoned in Shari'ah, and what is abandoned in Shari'ah is in the position of what is abandoned in custom. Do you not see that if someone swears 'La ukallimu hadha al-sabiyya' (I will not speak to this child), the oath is not restricted to the time of his childhood, because abandoning the child (i.e., not speaking to him) is abandoned in Shari'ah.'

Translation: On this basis we said that appointing an agent for litigation turns to absolute answer, because the literal meaning is abandoned in Shari'ah, and what is abandoned in Shari'ah is in the position of what is abandoned customarily. Do you not see that if someone swears that he will not speak to this child, the oath will not be restricted to the time of his childhood? Because abandoning the child, i.e., not speaking to him, is abandoned in Shari'ah.

Explanation: Above the principle was explained that when the literal meaning is abandoned (mahjoor), the metaphorical meaning is taken. The author mentions two examples.

First example: Zayd claimed a thousand against Khalid, so Zayd is the plaintiff and Khalid is the defendant. Now Khalid appointed Rashid as his agent for litigation. So Rashid is an agent for litigation. The literal meaning of being an agent for litigation is that the agent supports his client (Khalid) and defends him, whether right or wrong, only denying and not admitting. But this literal meaning is abandoned in Shari'ah, because unjust partisanship is not permissible in Shari'ah. And what is abandoned in Shari'ah is in the position of what is abandoned in custom, because it is far from a Muslim's dignity to make a habit and practice what is not permissible in Shari'ah; rather, he will leave it. So when the literal meaning is abandoned in Shari'ah, the metaphorical meaning will be intended. The metaphorical meaning of agency for litigation is that the agent gives an absolute answer—responds to the plaintiff's claim with 'yes' or 'no'—i.e., by saying 'yes' he affirms the claim and makes the thousand due upon his client, or by saying 'no' he rejects the claim and denies the thousand upon his client. So the metaphorical meaning is that the agent does not support his client but gives an absolute answer, whether in agreement with him or against him. And that is the meaning intended here.

Second example: Someone swore 'La ukallimu hadha al-sabiyya' (I will not speak to this child). The literal meaning of this statement is that the oath is restricted to the time of his childhood, i.e., I will not speak while he is a child. But this meaning is abandoned in Shari'ah, because the Hadith says: 'He who does not have mercy on our young is not from us.' Thus it is known that abandoning connection with a child is haram, and what is abandoned in Shari'ah is in the position of what is abandoned in custom. Therefore, the literal meaning is not intended; rather, the metaphorical meaning is intended. The metaphorical meaning is that the child's very essence is intended—i.e., I will not speak to his essence—so whether in childhood, youth, or old age, whenever he speaks, he will break his oath, because the essence exists at all times. So here, due to the literal meaning being abandoned, the metaphorical meaning is taken. But this is when he said 'hadha al-sabiyya'; if he said 'sabiyyan', then the oath would be restricted to his childhood.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a foundational usul al-fiqh rule: the priority of haqiqah (literal meaning) over majaz (metaphorical meaning) in legal interpretation. The author first states the general principle: as long as the literal meaning is possible, it must be acted upon; the metaphorical meaning is only resorted to when the literal is impossible (mut'adhir) or abandoned (mahjoor) either by custom ('urf) or by Shari'ah. He then provides detailed examples from oaths and agency contracts.

The key distinction is between 'impossible' (mut'adhir) and 'abandoned' (mahjoor). Impossible means the literal cannot be fulfilled without extreme difficulty; abandoned means the literal is possible but no longer used by people or by the Law. In the oath example of the date palm tree, eating the tree itself is impossible, so the metaphorical meaning (fruit or value) is taken. In the foot-in-house example, the literal meanings (barefoot, walking, owning) are abandoned in custom for the purpose of oaths, so absolute entry is intended.

The author then extends this rule to two important legal cases: (1) Agency for litigation (wakalah bil khusoomah): The literal meaning of an agent defending his client blindly (partisanship) is abandoned in Shari'ah because it is unjust; thus the agent is authorized to give an absolute response (admit or deny) without bias. (2) Oath regarding a child: The literal meaning restricted to childhood is abandoned because Shari'ah commands mercy towards children; thus the oath applies to the child's identity at any age. Both applications rely on the principle that what is abandoned in Shari'ah is treated like what is abandoned in custom.

The commentary follows the author's order: it states the rule, explains the two exceptions, gives examples from oaths, and then provides two additional examples from legal practice (wakalah and oath about child). The reasoning is consistently Hanafi, relying on the primacy of literal meaning unless overcome by impossibility or abandonment. The hadith about mercy on the young is cited to show the Shari'ah's abandonment of the literal restriction.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as used in its primary sense.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical or secondary meaning, used when literal meaning is impossible or abandoned.
متعذر (mut'adhir): Impossible to act upon literally without extreme difficulty or impossibility.
مهجور (mahjoor): Abandoned in custom or in Shari'ah; the literal meaning is possible but not used.
توکیل بالخصومة (tawkeel bil khusoomah): Agency for litigation; the agent is appointed to represent a party in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul rule discussed, when is metaphorical meaning (majaz) taken over literal meaning (haqiqah)?
A) Whenever the literal meaning seems less logical.
B) Only when the literal meaning is impossible or abandoned in custom/Shari'ah.
C) Only when the speaker explicitly states it.
D) In all cases of oaths and contracts.

Key Takeaway
Haqiqah is the default in Hanafi interpretation; majaz is only employed when literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, ensuring legal texts are applied sensibly and justly.

Preferring Literal or Metaphorical Meaning in Oaths: The Hanafi Dispute
Presenting a Hanafi usul dispute with oath examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on preferring literal vs. metaphorical meaning and apply it to determine oath-breaking in given scenarios.

Core Idea
When a word has both common literal and metaphorical meanings, Abu Hanifa prefers the literal, but the Sahibayn prefer the metaphorical.

In Hanafi usul, interpreting a word in an oath can be contentious when the word has a common literal meaning and an equally common metaphorical meaning. This lesson explores the internal Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), on which meaning takes precedence. Through concrete examples of oaths involving wheat and river water, you will see how this linguistic principle directly affects the ruling on whether the oath is broken.

Guided Path
1
Define the key terms
Understand 'haqiqah musta'malah' (common literal meaning) and 'majaz muta'araf' (common metaphorical meaning). The dispute only arises when both are equally common in everyday language.
The text uses 'musta'malah' for literal and 'muta'araf' for metaphorical, both indicating common usage.
2
State the dispute and positions
Identify that Imam Abu Hanifa holds 'al-'amal bi al-haqiqah awla' (acting on the literal is preferable) while the Sahibayn hold 'al-'amal bi 'umum al-majaz awla' (acting on the generality of the metaphorical is preferable).
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab one.
3
Examine the reasoning of each side
Abu Hanifa argues that the literal is the original (asl) and must be given precedence unless there is a strong reason to shift. The Sahibayn argue that the metaphorical meaning is more inclusive and safer for avoiding sin in oaths.
The Sahibayn's cautious approach aims to prevent accidental perjury by covering both meanings.
4
Apply to the wheat oath example
Take the oath 'I will not eat from this wheat' (hintah). Under Abu Hanifa, only eating raw wheat breaks the oath; under the Sahibayn, both raw wheat and bread break it, because the metaphorical 'wheat' includes wheat products. Note that barley gruel (sattu) is an exception even for the Sahibayn.
This thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is the key difference.
5
Apply to the river oath example
Take the oath 'I will not drink from this river' (furat). Under Abu Hanifa, only drinking directly from the river breaks the oath; under the Sahibayn, both direct drinking and drinking from a vessel break it.
The same logic applies: the metaphorical use of 'drinking from the river' includes any consumption of the river's water.

فَإِنْ كَانَ اللَّفْظُ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ مُسْتَعْمَلَةٌ وَمَجَانٌ مُتَعَارَفْ كَمَا إِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَا كُلُ مِنْ هذِهِ الْحِنْطَةِ أَوْلَا يَشْرَبْ مِنْ هَذِهِ الْفُرَاتِ فَعِنْدَابِي حَنِيفَةَ الْعَمَلُ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ أوْلى وَعِنْدَهُمَا الْعَمَلُ بِعْمُوْم الْمَجَازِ اولى ترجمہ :۔ پس اگر لفظ کے لئے حقیقت مستعملہ اور مجاز متعارف ہو، جیسے کہ جب قسم کھائی کہ وہ نہیں کھائے گا اس گیہوں سے ، یا نہیں پیئے گا اس فرات سے، تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک حقیقت پر عمل کرنا اولی ہے اور صاحبین کے ، نزدیک عموم مجاز پر عمل کرنا اولی ہے ریح:- مصنف عالی ایک اختلافی مسئلہ بیان کر رہے ہیں کہ ایک لفظ ایسا ہے کہ اس کے حقیقی معنی بھی مستعمل ہیں، اور اس کے لئے مجاز متعارف بھی ہے، تو اس وقت لفظ سے کون سے معنی مراد لئے جائیں گے ، امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزد یک حقیقی معنی مراد لینا زیادہ بہتر اور اولی ہے، اور صاحبین کہتے ہیں کہ مجاز متعارف زیادہ بہتر ہے ، مصنف نے اس کی دومثالیں بیان کی ہیں ۔ پہلی مثال: کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ اس گیہوں سے نہیں کھائے گا، تو اس کے حقیقی معنی خود گیہوں کو کھانا ، اور یہ معنی مستعمل ہیں ، لوگ گیہوں کو بھون کر اور اُبال کر کھاتے ہیں، اور مجازی معنی گیہوں سے بنی چیز روٹی وغیرہ کھانا ہے، اور یہ معنی بھی متعارف ہیں، جیسے محاورہ ہے کہ فلاں علاقہ کے لوگ گیہوں زیادہ کھاتے ہیں، مطلب روٹی کھاتے ہیں ، تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک حقیقی معنی مراد ہوں گے، کیونکہ حقیقت مستعمل ہے ،لہذا گیہوں بھون کر یا ابال کر کھائے گا تو حانث ہو جائے گا، روٹی کھانے سے حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ اور صاحبین کے نزدیک مجاز متعارف مراد ہوگا ، لہذا گیہوں کھانے سے بھی اور روٹی کھانے سے بھی حانث ہو جائے گا کیونکہ مجازی معنی ایسے عام ہیں کہ حقیقت بھی اس میں داخل ہے، البتہ ستو کھانے سے حانث نہ ہوگا کیونکہ ستو عرف میں دوسری جنس ہے۔ دوسری مثال: کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ اس نہر سے نہیں پئے گا، تو اس کے حقیقی معنی ہیں ، نہر سے منہ لگا کر پینا اور یہ مستعمل ہے ، دیہاتوں میں لوگ نہر سے منہ لگا کر پیتے ہیں، اور مجازی معنی ہے چلو سے پینا یا برتن سے پینا، اور یہ معنی بھی متعارف ہے، تو امام صاحب نے حقیقی معنی مراد لئے ، لہذا منہ لگا کر پینے سے حانث ہو جائے گا، برتن یا چلو سے پینے سے حانث نہ ہوگا۔
If the word has a common literal meaning and a common metaphorical meaning, like when someone swears that he will not eat from this wheat (hintah) or will not drink from this river (furat), then according to Abu Hanifa, acting on the literal meaning is preferable, and according to them (the two disciples), acting on the generality of the metaphorical meaning is preferable. Translation: So if a word has a common literal meaning and a common metaphorical meaning, as when he swears that he will not eat from this wheat or will not drink from this river, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, acting on the literal meaning is better and preferable, and according to the Sahibayn, acting on the generality of the metaphorical meaning is preferable. Explanation: The author is presenting a disputed issue: a word that has both a common literal meaning and a common metaphorical meaning; then which meaning is intended? According to Imam Abu Hanifa, taking the literal meaning is better and preferable, and according to the Sahibayn, the common metaphorical meaning is better. The author gives two examples. First example: Someone swore that he will not eat from this wheat. Its literal meaning is eating the wheat itself, and this meaning is common; people roast and boil wheat and eat it. The metaphorical meaning is eating something made from wheat, like bread, etc., and this meaning is also common, as in the expression 'the people of such area eat a lot of wheat', meaning they eat bread. So according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), the literal meaning is intended; therefore, if he roasts or boils wheat and eats it, he breaks his oath; if he eats bread, he does not break his oath. According to the Sahibayn, the common metaphorical meaning is intended; therefore, he breaks his oath whether he eats wheat or bread, because the metaphorical meaning is so general that the literal is included in it. However, eating barley gruel (sattu) does not break the oath because barley is a different type in custom. Second example: Someone swore that he will not drink from this river. Its literal meaning is drinking by putting the mouth to the river, and this is common; in villages, people drink by putting their mouth to the river. The metaphorical meaning is drinking from a ladle or a vessel, and this meaning is also common. So Imam (Abu Hanifa) intended the literal meaning; therefore, drinking by putting the mouth to the river breaks the oath, but drinking from a vessel or ladle does not break the oath.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi usul dispute regarding a word that has both a common literal meaning (haqiqah musta'malah) and a common metaphorical meaning (majaz muta'araf). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the literal meaning is preferable (al-'amal bi al-haqiqah awla). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that acting on the generality of the metaphorical meaning is preferable (al-'amal bi 'umum al-majaz awla). The author provides two examples from oaths. First, an oath not to eat 'hintah' (wheat): literal meaning = the raw grain; metaphorical = bread or wheat products. Under Abu Hanifa, only eating the raw grain breaks the oath; under the Sahibayn, both raw grain and bread break the oath, because the metaphorical meaning encompasses the literal. Second, an oath not to drink from a 'furat' (river): literal = drinking directly from the river; metaphorical = drinking from a vessel. Under Abu Hanifa, only direct drinking breaks the oath; under the Sahibayn, both direct and vessel drinking break the oath. The passage also notes exceptions like 'sattu' (barley gruel) which is a different custom and thus does not break the oath even under the Sahibayn's view.

Key Terms
حقیقت مستعملة (haqiqah musta'malah): A literal meaning that is commonly used and understood by people in everyday speech.
مجاز متعارف (majaz muta'araf): A metaphorical meaning that is commonly recognized and used in the language (muta'araf = conventionally known).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in the wheat oath example, under which view does eating bread break the oath?
A) Only under Imam Abu Hanifa's view
B) Only under the Sahibayn's view
C) Under both views
D) Under neither view

Key Takeaway
The preference for literal vs. metaphorical meaning in oaths is a settled intra-Hanafi dispute with clear practical consequences for determining oath-breaking.

Abandoning Haqiqi to Avoid Falsehood in Divine Speech
First scenario: abandoning haqiqi to avoid kadhib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a literal meaning must be abandoned to avoid falsehood in Quranic or Hadith texts.

Core Idea
If the literal meaning entails falsehood (kadhib) in divine speech, the figurative is obligatory.

This lesson focuses on the first scenario from Al-Faid al-Hijazi: when taking the literal meaning of a Quranic verse or hadith would lead to a false statement. Since falsehood is impossible in Allah’s speech, we must interpret figuratively. For example, 'The blind and the seeing are not equal' – literal equality in all things is false; figurative equality in sight is intended.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the default: haqiqah is primary
Assume the literal meaning unless a valid reason exists to abandon it.
Hanafi usul holds haqiqah as the original (asl).
2
Identify the consequence: kadhib
Check if the literal reading results in a false statement (kadhib) – impossible in divine speech.
Both Quran and Sunnah are free from falsehood.
3
Apply the rule: transfer to majaz
Since kadhib is impossible, the literal meaning is abandoned and a figurative meaning is sought that avoids falsehood.
The figurative meaning must be supported by a contextual clue (qarinah).
4
Verify with examples
Work through the examples: 'The blind and the seeing are not equal' (Quran) and 'Actions are but by intentions' (hadith).
In the hadith, literal meaning (actions exist only by intention) is false; figurative (reward requires intention) is intended.

نیت کے نہیں ملتا ہے، عمل تو ہو جاتا ہے۔ دوسری جگہ : دلالت عادت کی وجہ سے یعنی لفظ جب بولا جاتا ہے تو عادہ اور عرف میں اس کے حقیقی معنی مراد نہیں ہوتے بلکہ مجازی معنی مراد ہوتے ہیں، لہذا دلالت عادت کی وجہ سے حقیقی معنی متروک ہوں گے مگر یہ بات ذہن میں رہے کہ دلالت کی وجہ سے حقیقی معنی اس وقت متروک ہوں گے، جب حقیقی معنی مستعمل نہ ہوں، ورنہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک مجاز کے مقابلہ میں حقیقت مستعملہ پر عمل شوہر کا مقصد نہیں ہے، لہذا اس وقت اگر عورت نکلی تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی ، اور غصہ ٹھنڈا ہونے کے بعد نکلی تو طلاق واقع نہ ہوگی۔ خصیصی۔ چوتھی جگہ : سیاق کلام کی دلالت کی وجہ سے حقیقی معنی چھوڑ دئے جاتے ہیں، یعنی کلام میں ایسا کوئی لفظی قرینہ ہوتا ہے جو دلالت کرتا ہے کہ یہاں حقیقی معنی مراد نہیں ہے جیسے اللہ تعالی کا ارشاد ” جو چاہے ایمان لائے اور جو چاہے کفر کرے، بے شک ہم نے ظالموں کے لئے آگ تیار کر رکھی ہے اس کلام کے حقیقی معنی ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ ایمان و کفر میں اختیار دے رہے ہیں، یعنی چا ہو تو ایمان لاؤ، چاہو تو کفر کرو، دونوں کا اختیار ہے۔ مگر یہ معنی مراد نہیں ہے کیونکہ کلام میں اگلا جملہ انا اعتدنا للكافرين قرینہ ہے، جو بتلا رہا ہے کہ یہاں حقیقی معنی مراد نہیں ہے، بلکہ مجازی معنی مراد ہے یعنی زجر و تو یخ مقصود ہے۔ پانچویں جگہ: کبھی لفظ کی ذاتی دلالت کی وجہ سے حقیقی معنی چھوڑ دئے جاتے ہیں یعنی لفظ اپنے مادہ اور ماخذ اشتقاق کے لحاظ سے جس معنی پر دلالت کرتا ہے وہ معنی اس میں نہیں پائے جاتے ، اس لئے حقیقی معنی چھوڑ دیے ، تو یہ لفظ کی ذاتی دلالت ہوئی مثلاً لفظ اپنے مادے اور اشتقاق کے لحاظ سے ایسے معنی کے لئے وضع ہوا ہے، جس میں قوت اور شدت کے معنی ہیں، تو اس کے افراد میں سے وہ نکل جائینگے جس میں یہ معنی ناقص ہیں ، ایسے ہی کوئی لفظ ایسے معنی کے لئے وضع کیا گیا ہے جس میں ضعف اور نقصان کے معنی ہیں، تو اس کے افراد میں سے وہ نکل جائیں گے جن میں یہ معنی زیادتی کے ساتھ پائے جاتے ہیں۔ الحاصل لفظ اپنی وضع کے اعتبار سے تو اپنے تمام افراد کو شامل ہے مگر اپنے مادے اور ماخذ اشتقاق کی وجہ سے صرف ان ہی افراد کو شامل رہے گا جن میں یہ معنی جس پر یہ لفظ اپنے مادے اور ماخذ اشتقاق سے دلالت کرتا ہے برابر درجہ کے ہوں ، اور جن افراد میں وہ معنی کمی کے ساتھ یا زیادتی کے ساتھ پائے جائینگے وہ افراد خارج ہو جائیں گے۔ مثال سے سمجھو، کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ وہ گوشت نہیں کھائے گا ، اگر اس نے مچھلی کا گوشت کھالیا، تو حانث نہ ہوگا کیونکہ خود لفظ کی دلالت سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ مچھلی کا گوشت اس میں داخل نہیں ہے، کیونکہ تم التحام سے بنا ہے اور اس میں شدت کے معنی ہیں، اور مچھلی کے گوشت میں خون نہ ہونے کہ سبب شدت نہیں ہے، لہذا مچھلی خارج ہو جائے گی
Al-Faid al-Hijazi, Urdu commentary on al-Muntakhab al-Husami. They will intend literal meaning. For example: "The blind and the seeing are not equal, the inhabitants of the Fire and the inhabitants of Paradise are not equal." The literal meaning of this speech is that there is no equality between the two in anything, but this is not the case; rather, there is no equality in sight and lack of sight; besides that, in many attributes like being human, being rational, etc., there is equality. So denying equality in all attributes would necessitate falsehood. Similarly, between the inhabitant of Paradise and the inhabitant of the Fire there is equality in many attributes, so denying equality in all attributes would necessitate falsehood, and falsehood is impossible in Allah's speech. So the figurative meaning—denial of equality in some attributes—is intended. Similarly, in the hadith: "Actions are but by intentions." Literal meaning: actions come into existence by intentions. This is literal meaning, yet actions do occur without intention. So taking literal meaning would necessitate falsehood in the hadith, so we turn the speech to figurative meaning: reward for actions is not obtained without intention; the action does occur. Second place: due to indication of custom. That is, when a word is spoken, in custom and usage its literal meaning is not intended but figurative meaning is intended. So due to indication of custom, literal meaning is abandoned. But bear in mind that literal meaning is abandoned only when its literal meaning is not in current use; otherwise, according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], using the literal meaning in common usage takes precedence over figurative, as established previously. Example: Salah, Sawm, Hajj, etc. The literal meaning of Salah is supplication, but in custom and usage these meanings are abandoned and figurative meanings are intended, i.e., prayer. Similarly, the literal meaning of Sawm is to refrain; figurative: specific worship. Literal meaning of Hajj is to intend; figurative: visiting the House of Allah with specific rites. Similarly, when someone says: "I will not eat from this tree," "I will not step foot in so-and-so's house," etc.—all these are examples whose details have passed. Third place: due to indication of a meaning that refers back to the speaker. That is, meanings arise in the speaker that indicate abandoning literal meaning, and it becomes apparent that the speaker does not intend literal meaning. Then also literal meaning is abandoned and figurative meaning is intended. For example, a "yamin fawri"—an oath taken in anger. It is called so because it occurs in a rush of anger with no pause. Like when a husband and wife quarrel and the wife starts to leave the house; the husband says in anger: "If you go out, you are divorced." Hearing this, the woman stops. After half an hour when her anger cools and she goes out, divorce does not occur because the literal meaning is general (whenever she goes out, divorce), and the figurative meaning is the specific time of exit. So here the figurative meaning is intended, not the literal, because the meaning of anger that arose in the speaker at the time of her departure indicates that this specific exit is intended; his anger is a contextual clue (qarinah) that absolute exit is not his aim. So if she goes out at that time, divorce occurs, but if she goes out after the anger subsides, divorce does not occur. Subtle point. Fourth place: due to the indication of the context of speech (siyaq al-kalam). That is, there is some verbal clue (qarinah lafzi) in the speech that indicates that the literal meaning is not intended here. For example, Allah's saying: "Whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve. Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire." The literal meaning of this speech is that Allah is giving choice in faith and disbelief—if you wish, believe, if you wish, disbelieve, both are optional. But this meaning is not intended because the next sentence "Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire" is a clue indicating that literal meaning is not intended but figurative meaning is intended, i.e., threat and deterrence. Fifth place: sometimes due to the intrinsic indication of the word (dalalah dhatiyyah) literal meaning is abandoned. That is, the word, in terms of its root and derivation, indicates a meaning that is not fully present; thus literal meaning is abandoned. This is the word's intrinsic indication. For example, a word was coined for a meaning that implies strength and intensity; then among its referents, those in which this meaning is deficient will be excluded. Similarly, a word coined for a meaning implying weakness and deficiency; then among its referents, those in which this meaning is excessive will be excluded. In sum, according to its coinage, the word includes all its referents, but due to its root and derivation, it will include only those referents in which the meaning indicated by the root and derivation is of an equal degree; those referents in which that meaning is found deficiently or excessively will be excluded. Understand by example: someone swore that he will not eat meat (lahm). If he ate fish meat, he does not become sinful (hanith) because the word's own indication shows that fish meat is not included, because the word is derived from "tam ul-lahm" and it implies intensity, and fish meat does not have intensity due to lack of blood, so fish is excluded.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of five scenarios in which the literal (haqiqi) meaning of a word is abandoned in favor of the figurative (majazi) meaning. The author, commenting on al-Husami, provides examples from the Quran, hadith, and everyday oaths. The five scenarios are: 1) when the literal meaning would lead to falsehood (kadhib) in divine speech or prophetic hadith; 2) when customary usage (urf/ada) has rendered the literal meaning obsolete and the figurative is intended; 3) when a state of the speaker (such as anger) provides a contextual clue (qarinah) that limits the literal general meaning; 4) when the context of the speech (siyaq al-kalam) contains a verbal clue that shifts the meaning; and 5) when the word's etymological derivation (ishtiqaq) implies a quality (like intensity) that is absent in some referents, so those are excluded. The commentary follows the order of the text, explaining each scenario with its example. Note that for scenario 2, the author emphasizes that if the literal meaning is still in use, it takes precedence over the figurative according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
حقیقت (haqiqah): The original literal meaning for which a word was coined.
مجاز (majaz): The figurative meaning intended when the literal is abandoned due to a clue.
قرینہ (qarinah): A contextual clue (verbal or situational) that indicates the figurative meaning is intended.
کذب (kadhib): Falsehood; a statement that does not match reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the primary reason to abandon the literal meaning in the first scenario?
A) The literal meaning is too general.
B) The literal meaning leads to falsehood (kadhib).
C) The words are ambiguous.
D) The custom of the Arabs requires figurative speech.

Key Takeaway
When literal interpretation of a divine text would result in falsehood, the figurative meaning must be taken to maintain the truthfulness of revelation.

beginner
7 min
Abandoning Haqiqi Due to Customary Usage (Dalalah al-Adah)
Second scenario: abandoning haqiqi due to 'urf/adah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that customary usage (urf) can override the literal meaning of a word when the literal is no longer in common use.

Core Idea
If custom abandons the literal meaning, the figurative meaning becomes the default.

The second scenario involves words whose literal meanings are no longer used in everyday speech. For example, 'salah' literally means supplication but in Islamic law it refers to the ritual prayer. Customary usage (dalalah al-adah) indicates that the figurative meaning is intended. However, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that if the literal meaning is still in use, it takes precedence over figurative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word in question
Look for a word that has both a literal (linguistic) meaning and a figurative (legal) meaning.
Examples: salah (dua vs prayer), sawm (to refrain vs fasting), hajj (to intend vs pilgrimage).
2
Determine current custom (urf)
Check whether the literal meaning is still commonly used in the language or has become obsolete.
If the literal meaning is obsolete, the figurative is the intended meaning.
3
Apply the hierarchy: haqiqah not in use -> majaz
If the literal meaning is no longer used in customary speech (urf), the word is taken figuratively. If still in use, haqiqah is preferred.
This is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa as stated in the text.
4
Confirm with examples
Work through salah, sawm, hajj, and everyday oaths like 'I will not eat from this tree' (where 'eat' may be figurative for eating fruit).
In oaths, if custom uses a word figuratively, the oath is interpreted figuratively.

کیونکہ تم التحام سے بنا ہے اور اس میں شدت کے معنی ہیں، اور مچھلی کے گوشت میں خون نہ ہونے کہ سبب شدت نہیں ہے، لہذا مچھلی خارج ہو جائے گی
Al-Faid al-Hijazi, Urdu commentary on al-Muntakhab al-Husami. They will intend literal meaning. For example: "The blind and the seeing are not equal, the inhabitants of the Fire and the inhabitants of Paradise are not equal." The literal meaning of this speech is that there is no equality between the two in anything, but this is not the case; rather, there is no equality in sight and lack of sight; besides that, in many attributes like being human, being rational, etc., there is equality. So denying equality in all attributes would necessitate falsehood. Similarly, between the inhabitant of Paradise and the inhabitant of the Fire there is equality in many attributes, so denying equality in all attributes would necessitate falsehood, and falsehood is impossible in Allah's speech. So the figurative meaning—denial of equality in some attributes—is intended. Similarly, in the hadith: "Actions are but by intentions." Literal meaning: actions come into existence by intentions. This is literal meaning, yet actions do occur without intention. So taking literal meaning would necessitate falsehood in the hadith, so we turn the speech to figurative meaning: reward for actions is not obtained without intention; the action does occur. Second place: due to indication of custom. That is, when a word is spoken, in custom and usage its literal meaning is not intended but figurative meaning is intended. So due to indication of custom, literal meaning is abandoned. But bear in mind that literal meaning is abandoned only when its literal meaning is not in current use; otherwise, according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], using the literal meaning in common usage takes precedence over figurative, as established previously. Example: Salah, Sawm, Hajj, etc. The literal meaning of Salah is supplication, but in custom and usage these meanings are abandoned and figurative meanings are intended, i.e., prayer. Similarly, the literal meaning of Sawm is to refrain; figurative: specific worship. Literal meaning of Hajj is to intend; figurative: visiting the House of Allah with specific rites. Similarly, when someone says: "I will not eat from this tree," "I will not step foot in so-and-so's house," etc.—all these are examples whose details have passed. Third place: due to indication of a meaning that refers back to the speaker. That is, meanings arise in the speaker that indicate abandoning literal meaning, and it becomes apparent that the speaker does not intend literal meaning. Then also literal meaning is abandoned and figurative meaning is intended. For example, a "yamin fawri"—an oath taken in anger. It is called so because it occurs in a rush of anger with no pause. Like when a husband and wife quarrel and the wife starts to leave the house; the husband says in anger: "If you go out, you are divorced." Hearing this, the woman stops. After half an hour when her anger cools and she goes out, divorce does not occur because the literal meaning is general (whenever she goes out, divorce), and the figurative meaning is the specific time of exit. So here the figurative meaning is intended, not the literal, because the meaning of anger that arose in the speaker at the time of her departure indicates that this specific exit is intended; his anger is a contextual clue (qarinah) that absolute exit is not his aim. So if she goes out at that time, divorce occurs, but if she goes out after the anger subsides, divorce does not occur. Subtle point. Fourth place: due to the indication of the context of speech (siyaq al-kalam). That is, there is some verbal clue (qarinah lafzi) in the speech that indicates that the literal meaning is not intended here. For example, Allah's saying: "Whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve. Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire." The literal meaning of this speech is that Allah is giving choice in faith and disbelief—if you wish, believe, if you wish, disbelieve, both are optional. But this meaning is not intended because the next sentence "Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire" is a clue indicating that literal meaning is not intended but figurative meaning is intended, i.e., threat and deterrence. Fifth place: sometimes due to the intrinsic indication of the word (dalalah dhatiyyah) literal meaning is abandoned. That is, the word, in terms of its root and derivation, indicates a meaning that is not fully present; thus literal meaning is abandoned. This is the word's intrinsic indication. For example, a word was coined for a meaning that implies strength and intensity; then among its referents, those in which this meaning is deficient will be excluded. Similarly, a word coined for a meaning implying weakness and deficiency; then among its referents, those in which this meaning is excessive will be excluded. In sum, according to its coinage, the word includes all its referents, but due to its root and derivation, it will include only those referents in which the meaning indicated by the root and derivation is of an equal degree; those referents in which that meaning is found deficiently or excessively will be excluded. Understand by example: someone swore that he will not eat meat (lahm). If he ate fish meat, he does not become sinful (hanith) because the word's own indication shows that fish meat is not included, because the word is derived from "tam ul-lahm" and it implies intensity, and fish meat does not have intensity due to lack of blood, so fish is excluded.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of five scenarios in which the literal (haqiqi) meaning of a word is abandoned in favor of the figurative (majazi) meaning. The author, commenting on al-Husami, provides examples from the Quran, hadith, and everyday oaths. The five scenarios are: 1) when the literal meaning would lead to falsehood (kadhib) in divine speech or prophetic hadith; 2) when customary usage (urf/ada) has rendered the literal meaning obsolete and the figurative is intended; 3) when a state of the speaker (such as anger) provides a contextual clue (qarinah) that limits the literal general meaning; 4) when the context of the speech (siyaq al-kalam) contains a verbal clue that shifts the meaning; and 5) when the word's etymological derivation (ishtiqaq) implies a quality (like intensity) that is absent in some referents, so those are excluded. The commentary follows the order of the text, explaining each scenario with its example. Note that for scenario 2, the author emphasizes that if the literal meaning is still in use, it takes precedence over the figurative according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
حقیقت (haqiqah): The original literal meaning for which a word was coined.
مجاز (majaz): The figurative meaning intended when the literal is abandoned due to a clue.
دلالت عادت (dalalah al-'adah): Indication based on customary usage; when a word is no longer used in its literal sense, the figurative is intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when does customary usage override the literal meaning?
A) Always, because custom is stronger.
B) Only when the literal meaning is not in current use.
C) Only when the figurative meaning is more common.
D) Never; literal always prevails.

Key Takeaway
Customary usage (urf) can shift meaning from literal to figurative, but only if the literal has fallen out of common use.

Abandoning Haqiqi Due to the Speaker's State (Ma'na al-Mutakallim)
Third scenario: abandoning haqiqi due to speaker's state

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a speaker's emotional state (like anger) serves as a contextual clue (qarinah) to interpret an oath figuratively rather than literally.

Core Idea
The speaker's state can limit a general literal meaning to a specific figurative one.

The third scenario involves when the speaker's condition (such as anger) provides a qarinah that the literal meaning is not intended. For example, a husband in anger says 'If you go out, you are divorced.' The literal meaning covers all future exits, but the anger indicates he meant only the immediate exit. This is called yamin fawri (sudden oath).

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning of the statement
Take the apparent general meaning of the speaker's words.
Example: 'If you go out, you are divorced' – literally, divorce occurs on any exit.
2
Detect the speaker's state (qarinah haliyyah)
Look for emotional or circumstantial clues that indicate the speaker did not intend the literal scope.
Anger is a common qarinah haliyyah.
3
Shift to figurative meaning
Since the qarinah shows the literal meaning is not intended, interpret the statement figuratively – e.g., apply only to the specific exit in the moment.
The figurative meaning must be supported by the qarinah.
4
Apply to the example
In the husband's angry oath, if the wife leaves during the anger, divorce occurs; if she leaves later when anger is gone, no divorce.
This illustrates the effect: the figurative meaning is tied to the moment of anger.

کیونکہ تم التحام سے بنا ہے اور اس میں شدت کے معنی ہیں، اور مچھلی کے گوشت میں خون نہ ہونے کہ سبب شدت نہیں ہے، لہذا مچھلی خارج ہو جائے گی
Al-Faid al-Hijazi, Urdu commentary on al-Muntakhab al-Husami. They will intend literal meaning. For example: "The blind and the seeing are not equal, the inhabitants of the Fire and the inhabitants of Paradise are not equal." The literal meaning of this speech is that there is no equality between the two in anything, but this is not the case; rather, there is no equality in sight and lack of sight; besides that, in many attributes like being human, being rational, etc., there is equality. So denying equality in all attributes would necessitate falsehood. Similarly, between the inhabitant of Paradise and the inhabitant of the Fire there is equality in many attributes, so denying equality in all attributes would necessitate falsehood, and falsehood is impossible in Allah's speech. So the figurative meaning—denial of equality in some attributes—is intended. Similarly, in the hadith: "Actions are but by intentions." Literal meaning: actions come into existence by intentions. This is literal meaning, yet actions do occur without intention. So taking literal meaning would necessitate falsehood in the hadith, so we turn the speech to figurative meaning: reward for actions is not obtained without intention; the action does occur. Second place: due to indication of custom. That is, when a word is spoken, in custom and usage its literal meaning is not intended but figurative meaning is intended. So due to indication of custom, literal meaning is abandoned. But bear in mind that literal meaning is abandoned only when its literal meaning is not in current use; otherwise, according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], using the literal meaning in common usage takes precedence over figurative, as established previously. Example: Salah, Sawm, Hajj, etc. The literal meaning of Salah is supplication, but in custom and usage these meanings are abandoned and figurative meanings are intended, i.e., prayer. Similarly, the literal meaning of Sawm is to refrain; figurative: specific worship. Literal meaning of Hajj is to intend; figurative: visiting the House of Allah with specific rites. Similarly, when someone says: "I will not eat from this tree," "I will not step foot in so-and-so's house," etc.—all these are examples whose details have passed. Third place: due to indication of a meaning that refers back to the speaker. That is, meanings arise in the speaker that indicate abandoning literal meaning, and it becomes apparent that the speaker does not intend literal meaning. Then also literal meaning is abandoned and figurative meaning is intended. For example, a "yamin fawri"—an oath taken in anger. It is called so because it occurs in a rush of anger with no pause. Like when a husband and wife quarrel and the wife starts to leave the house; the husband says in anger: "If you go out, you are divorced." Hearing this, the woman stops. After half an hour when her anger cools and she goes out, divorce does not occur because the literal meaning is general (whenever she goes out, divorce), and the figurative meaning is the specific time of exit. So here the figurative meaning is intended, not the literal, because the meaning of anger that arose in the speaker at the time of her departure indicates that this specific exit is intended; his anger is a contextual clue (qarinah) that absolute exit is not his aim. So if she goes out at that time, divorce occurs, but if she goes out after the anger subsides, divorce does not occur. Subtle point. Fourth place: due to the indication of the context of speech (siyaq al-kalam). That is, there is some verbal clue (qarinah lafzi) in the speech that indicates that the literal meaning is not intended here. For example, Allah's saying: "Whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve. Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire." The literal meaning of this speech is that Allah is giving choice in faith and disbelief—if you wish, believe, if you wish, disbelieve, both are optional. But this meaning is not intended because the next sentence "Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire" is a clue indicating that literal meaning is not intended but figurative meaning is intended, i.e., threat and deterrence. Fifth place: sometimes due to the intrinsic indication of the word (dalalah dhatiyyah) literal meaning is abandoned. That is, the word, in terms of its root and derivation, indicates a meaning that is not fully present; thus literal meaning is abandoned. This is the word's intrinsic indication. For example, a word was coined for a meaning that implies strength and intensity; then among its referents, those in which this meaning is deficient will be excluded. Similarly, a word coined for a meaning implying weakness and deficiency; then among its referents, those in which this meaning is excessive will be excluded. In sum, according to its coinage, the word includes all its referents, but due to its root and derivation, it will include only those referents in which the meaning indicated by the root and derivation is of an equal degree; those referents in which that meaning is found deficiently or excessively will be excluded. Understand by example: someone swore that he will not eat meat (lahm). If he ate fish meat, he does not become sinful (hanith) because the word's own indication shows that fish meat is not included, because the word is derived from "tam ul-lahm" and it implies intensity, and fish meat does not have intensity due to lack of blood, so fish is excluded.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of five scenarios in which the literal (haqiqi) meaning of a word is abandoned in favor of the figurative (majazi) meaning. The author, commenting on al-Husami, provides examples from the Quran, hadith, and everyday oaths. The five scenarios are: 1) when the literal meaning would lead to falsehood (kadhib) in divine speech or prophetic hadith; 2) when customary usage (urf/ada) has rendered the literal meaning obsolete and the figurative is intended; 3) when a state of the speaker (such as anger) provides a contextual clue (qarinah) that limits the literal general meaning; 4) when the context of the speech (siyaq al-kalam) contains a verbal clue that shifts the meaning; and 5) when the word's etymological derivation (ishtiqaq) implies a quality (like intensity) that is absent in some referents, so those are excluded. The commentary follows the order of the text, explaining each scenario with its example. Note that for scenario 2, the author emphasizes that if the literal meaning is still in use, it takes precedence over the figurative according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
یمین فوری (yamin fawri): A sudden oath taken in anger without reflection.
قرینہ حالیہ (qarinah haliyyah): A contextual clue derived from the speaker's emotional or physical state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the third scenario, what is the qarinah that leads to abandoning the literal meaning?
A) The words themselves are ambiguous.
B) The speaker's anger indicates he meant only the immediate exit.
C) The listener's interpretation.
D) The time of day.

Key Takeaway
A speaker's emotional state (like anger) can serve as a contextual clue to limit a general literal meaning to a specific figurative one.

Abandoning Haqiqi Due to Context of Speech (Siyaq al-Kalam)
Fourth scenario: abandoning haqiqi due to context (siyaq al-kalam)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a verbal clue (qarinah lafzi) in the context of speech shifts the meaning from literal to figurative.

Core Idea
The surrounding text can provide a verbal clue that overrides the literal meaning.

The fourth scenario involves a verbal qarinah within the speech itself that indicates the literal meaning is not intended. The Quranic verse 'Whoever wills let him believe, whoever wills let him disbelieve' literally seems to give choice, but the following clause 'We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire' is a qarinah that the verse is a threat, not a permission. Thus the figurative meaning (intimidation) is taken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent literal meaning
Read the text as it stands without considering broader context.
Example: 'Whoever wills let him believe, whoever wills let him disbelieve' – sounds like free choice.
2
Look for a verbal qarinah in the surrounding text
Check for subsequent or preceding phrases that contradict the literal meaning.
The next clause 'We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire' is a threat, not an offer of choice.
3
Determine that the literal meaning is not intended
The contradiction shows the literal meaning cannot be correct; therefore, figurative meaning is sought.
In this case, the figurative meaning is threat (zajr) and deterrence.
4
Apply to other examples
Practice with other texts where a verbal qarinah shifts meaning, e.g., 'Eat and drink until the white thread becomes clear to you from the black thread' – the qarinah 'of dawn' specifies 'thread' to mean 'true dawn.'
Context clarifies ambiguous or general terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
