مقام ہوتا ہے، اس لئے غلام حکم ملک میں ، اور بقاء اذن میں نہ کہ حکم تصرف میں وکیل کی طرح ہوگا یعنی غلام ملک ید یعنی قبضہ
He is also eligible that something be obligatory upon him; because of this dhimma, humans are distinguished from animals. The dhimma of a slave is deficient. Wilaya, i.e., making one's statement binding on others, a slave is not eligible for wilaya. Similarly, the permissibility of women (marriage) is also deficient for a slave: a free man can marry four women, a slave can only marry two. However, in otherworldly honor, slave and free are equal, because it is based on taqwa: 'Indeed the most noble of you in the sight of Allah is the most righteous' (49:13). Until that his dhimma - The author is explaining the deficiency due to slavery in the aforementioned three matters. He says that a slave, due to being human, has original dhimma established, but due to slavery it is weak. So because of deficient dhimma, that slave cannot bear debt by himself, i.e., if he is a slave not permitted to trade, he will not be demanded debt immediately, but after emancipation. Because due to slavery, he does not have the capacity for obligation of debt toward others. But since the original dhimma exists in the slave, if the value of his person and his earnings are combined with the dhimma, then the dhimma can bear debts. Combining the value of his person and earnings means that the master gives him permission for transactions. Then if the slave destroys someone's property or becomes indebted, he will pay compensation by earning, or he will be sold for the debt. So the master's permission to transact is combining the value of his person and earnings with his dhimma. And when this is combined with the dhimma, now the slave will bear debt because the essence of dhimma exists. Similarly, due to slavery, there is half-splitting in permissibility (marriage): a slave can marry two women, and a slave woman becomes irrevocably divorced (muhallaghah) by two divorces, whether her husband is free or slave. And the iddah of a slave woman is also half, i.e., two menstrual periods, while a free woman's iddah is three. And similarly, qasm (division of nights) is also a favor; in this too the right of a married slave woman is half. And similarly, hadd (punishment for disobedience) is penalty and chastisement; this is also half in slaves compared to free persons. In a free person, Allah's favor is complete, so punishment is also complete; in a slave, Allah's favor is deficient, so punishment and hadd are also deficient. 'And the value of his person is diminished' - The value of a slave's life is less than that of a free person due to slavery. So if someone accidentally kills a slave, the killer's 'aqilah (responsible relatives) will owe some less than ten thousand dirhams, even if the slave's price is more than ten thousand. And if the slave's price is less than ten thousand, the price is owed. The proof for diyah being less is that ownership (mālkiyyat) is of two types: (1) Ownership of disposal and possession in property and also ownership of the very thing (ayn). In the free person, this ownership is complete; in the slave it is deficient, because the slave is owner of possession and disposal but not owner of the very thing. (2) Ownership of other than property, such as ownership of usufruct (milk mut'ah) through marriage. This ownership is only acquired by the male, whether free or slave, and the woman cannot own it. In the free man, both types of ownership are complete, so his diyah is also complete. The woman has only the first type of ownership, so her diyah is half that of a man. In the slave, the first type is deficient and the second is complete, so his diyah is slightly less than that of a free man. 'And this is in our view' - The author says that the slave's eligibility for disposal in property is our school. Because the master has given him permission to transact, so his transaction is for himself as principal (aṣālah), not as the master's deputy. Therefore, from his transaction, the primary ruling of transaction, i.e., possession (qabḍ), belongs to him. As for not obtaining ownership of the very thing, i.e., the slave has become owner of possession and disposal but not of the very thing. So ownership of the very thing (milk 'ayn) is something additional to possession (milk yad). That is, the slave has become eligible for transaction and possession, but he did not become owner of it. So milk 'ayn is an additional thing; possession is fundamental, because by taking possession, a person can satisfy his need, even if he did not become owner. And if he became owner but did not obtain possession, he cannot benefit. Anyway, he himself is the owner of disposal and possession; in this the slave is a principal. And milk 'ayn, which is prescribed for reaching possession (milk yad), in that the master is the deputy of the permitted slave. Therefore, ownership is established for the master. In principle, property should also be established for the direct agent (the slave), but since the slave is not eligible for ownership, the master is made his deputy and ownership is established for the master. This passage is actually a response to Imam al-Shafi'i's argument. Imam al-Shafi'i's argument is that a slave is not eligible for disposal and possession, just as he is not eligible for ownership. Hence, the possession he has over his earnings is not his own possession but rather possession by trust (amānah), like a depositary, because if the slave were eligible for disposal and possession, he would also be eligible for ownership, since disposal is a cause of ownership, and the slave is unanimously not eligible for ownership, so he will not be eligible for disposal and possession. The response formulation is that the primary purpose of disposal is possession, and this is obtained by the slave. Possession is the primary purpose because by possession a person can benefit from the thing; there is no need for establishing ownership for that. Therefore, milk 'ayn is not necessary for this primary purpose of possession; rather it is a means to reach possession. So absence of eligibility for a particular means does not necessitate absence of the purpose; rather it is possible that there is another means for the purpose. 'And because of this we made the slave' - Since ownership is not established for the slave, rather the master stands in his place, therefore the slave, in the ruling of ownership and continuation of permission, not in the ruling of disposal, will be like a wakeel (agent). That is, the slave (has) milk yad (possession).
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage from the Urdu commentary on Al-Husami discusses the legal capacity (ahliyyah) of slaves according to the Hanafi school, contrasting it with that of free persons and addressing a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. The passage opens by explaining the concept of dhimma (legal responsibility), which is inherent in humans but weakened in slaves due to slavery (riqq). This deficiency affects several areas: (1) With respect to debts, a slave not permitted to trade cannot be held liable for debts until freed, but if the master grants permission (idhn), the slave's own person and earnings become available for debt satisfaction, allowing the slave to bear debts. (2) In marriage, a slave can marry only two women (half of the free man's capacity), and a slave woman's divorce occurs by two divorces instead of three; her iddah is two menstrual periods instead of three; and her right to division of nights is halved. (3) In criminal penalties (hudud), punishments are halved for slaves compared to free persons, mirroring the reduction in worldly favors. The author then turns to blood money (diyah) for accidental killing: the diyah for a slave is less than that of a free man, determined by a reduced amount (less than 10,000 dirhams) or the slave's actual value if lower. The reasoning is based on ownership powers: a free man has full ownership of both possession/disposal and the very thing; a woman has only the first; a slave has deficient first but complete second, so his diyah is slightly less than a free man's. The passage then shifts to a specific dispute: the Hanafi position that a slave permitted to trade (ma'dhun) can possess and transact, but does not own the thing itself. Imam al-Shafi'i argued that since the slave cannot own, he also cannot possess and transact, because transaction is a cause of ownership. The Hanafi response distinguishes between the purpose of possession (qabd) and ownership (milk ayn): possession is the primary purpose and serves to satisfy need, and it can exist without ownership. The slave's possession is valid because the master's permission makes the slave a principal in possession, while the master acts as deputy for ownership. Thus, the slave is analogous to a wakeel (agent) in matters of possession and continuation of permission, not in the substance of disposal. This commentary emphasizes the Hanafi commitment to istihsan (juristic preference) in allowing the slave's capacity for transaction despite the general incapacity for ownership.

Key Terms
ذمة (dhimma): Legal responsibility or capacity; the quality by which a person can be subject to rights and obligations.
رقية (riqqiyyah): Slavery; a legal status that reduces legal capacity.
ملکیت (milkiyyat): Ownership; classified into two types: milk yad (possession/disposal) and milk 'ayn (ownership of the very thing).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a slave's diyah less than a free man's but more than a woman's?
A) Because a slave has both types of ownership complete.
B) Because a slave's first type of ownership (disposal/possession) is deficient, while the second type (milk mut'ah) is complete, unlike a woman who lacks the second type.
C) Because a slave's dhimma is completely absent.
D) Because the Quran says so directly.

Key Takeaway
The deficient dhimma of a slave produces proportional reductions in legal capacity, which can be strengthened by the master's permission.

Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on the Ma'dhun Slave's Capacity for Possession
Refuting the Shafi'i argument that a ma'dhun slave cannot validly possess.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on a ma'dhun slave's capacity for possession (qabd) and disposal (tasarruf), and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the distinction between possession and ownership.

Core Idea
Possession fulfills need without ownership; a slave can possess and transact as principal, with the master as deputy for ownership.

Imam al-Shafi'i argued that since a slave cannot own property, he also cannot validly possess or transact—because transaction is the cause of ownership, and the effect cannot exist without the cause. The Hanafi school disagrees, drawing a sharp distinction between possession (qabd) and ownership (milk 'ayn). This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rebuttal, showing how the purpose of transaction is fulfilled by possession alone, and how the master's permission allows the slave to act as a principal in possession while the master stands as deputy for ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the point of dispute
The dispute is whether a ma'dhun slave can validly possess (qabd) and transact (tasarruf). Shafi'i says no; Hanafi says yes.
Both agree the slave cannot own (milk 'ayn).
2
Understand the Shafi'i argument
Shafi'i argues that disposal (tasarruf) is the cause of ownership (sabab al-milk). Since the slave cannot own, he cannot cause ownership through disposal. Thus his possession is merely a trust (amanah), not valid ownership.
Cause and effect are inseparable.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The primary purpose (maqsad) of disposal is possession (qabd), not ownership. Possession allows benefit, which meets the need. Ownership is an additional means (wasila) to possession, not the purpose itself.
Purpose and means are separable.
4
Apply the Hanafi framework
With the master's permission, the slave becomes a principal (asil) in possession while the master acts as deputy (na'ib) for ownership. This dual arrangement allows the slave to transact validly.
The slave is like a wakeel for possession, but the master is wakeel for ownership.

لینے سے آدمی ضرورت پوری کر سکتا ہے، چاہے وہ مالک نہ بنا ہو، اور اگر مالک تو بنا لیکن اس کو قبضہ نہ حاصل ہو تو فائدہ نہیں اٹھا سکتا ، بہر حال تصرف اور قبضہ کا مالک وہ خود ہے اس میں غلام اصیل ہے ، اور ملک عین جو ملک ید یعنی قبضہ تک رسائی کے لیے مشروع ہے، اس میں آقا عبد ماذون کا نائب ہوگا، لہذا ملک آقا کے لئے ثابت ہوگی ، اصل تو مباشر یعنی غلام کے لئے ملک بھی ثابت ہونی چاہیے مگر چونکہ غلام ملک کا اہل نہیں ہے اس لئے آقا کو اس کا نائب بنا کر ملک آقا کے لئے ثابت ہوگی۔ یہ عبارت دراصل امام شافعی کے استدلال کا جواب ہے، امام شافعی کا استدلال یہ ہے کہ غلام تصرف وقبضہ کا بھی اہل نہیں ہے ، جیسے ملک کا اہل نہیں ہے، لہذا اس کی کمائی پر جو اس کا قبضہ ہے وہ اس کا اپنا قبضہ نہیں ہے بلکہ قبضہ امانت ہے جیسے مودع یعنی جس کے پاس امانت رکھی جائے ، کیونکہ اگر غلام تصرف اور قبضہ کا اہل ہوتا تو ملک کا بھی اہل ہوتا، اس لئے کہ تصرف ملک کا سبب ہے، اور غلام بالاتفاق ملک کا اہل نہیں ہے لہذا تصرف وقبضہ کا بھی اہل نہ ہوگا۔ جواب کی تقریر یہ ہے کہ تصرف کا مقصد اصلی قبضہ ہے، اور یہ غلام کو حاصل ہے، اور قبضہ مقصد اصلی اس لئے ہے کہ قبضہ سے آدمی اس شمی سے فائدہ اٹھا سکتا ہے، اس کے لئے ملک کے ثبوت کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے، لہذا ملک عین اس مقصد اصلی یعنی قبضہ کے لئے لازم نہیں ہے، بلکہ وہ تو قبضہ تک پہنچنے کا ایک وسیلہ ہے، پس ایک خاص وسیلہ کی اہلیت نہ ہونے سے مقصود کا نہ ہونا لازم نہ آئے گا، بلکہ ممکن ہے کہ مقصود کے لئے کوئی دوسرا وسیلہ ہو۔ ولهذا جعلنا العبد : - چونکہ غلام کے لئے ملک ثابت نہیں ہوتی، بلکہ مولی غلام کے قائم مقام ہوتا ہے، اس لئے غلام حکم ملک میں ، اور بقاء اذن میں نہ کہ حکم تصرف میں وکیل کی طرح ہوگا یعنی غلام ملک ید یعنی قبضہ
He is also eligible that something be obligatory upon him; because of this dhimma, humans are distinguished from animals. The dhimma of a slave is deficient. Wilaya, i.e., making one's statement binding on others, a slave is not eligible for wilaya. Similarly, the permissibility of women (marriage) is also deficient for a slave: a free man can marry four women, a slave can only marry two. However, in otherworldly honor, slave and free are equal, because it is based on taqwa: 'Indeed the most noble of you in the sight of Allah is the most righteous' (49:13). Until that his dhimma - The author is explaining the deficiency due to slavery in the aforementioned three matters. He says that a slave, due to being human, has original dhimma established, but due to slavery it is weak. So because of deficient dhimma, that slave cannot bear debt by himself, i.e., if he is a slave not permitted to trade, he will not be demanded debt immediately, but after emancipation. Because due to slavery, he does not have the capacity for obligation of debt toward others. But since the original dhimma exists in the slave, if the value of his person and his earnings are combined with the dhimma, then the dhimma can bear debts. Combining the value of his person and earnings means that the master gives him permission for transactions. Then if the slave destroys someone's property or becomes indebted, he will pay compensation by earning, or he will be sold for the debt. So the master's permission to transact is combining the value of his person and earnings with his dhimma. And when this is combined with the dhimma, now the slave will bear debt because the essence of dhimma exists. Similarly, due to slavery, there is half-splitting in permissibility (marriage): a slave can marry two women, and a slave woman becomes irrevocably divorced (muhallaghah) by two divorces, whether her husband is free or slave. And the iddah of a slave woman is also half, i.e., two menstrual periods, while a free woman's iddah is three. And similarly, qasm (division of nights) is also a favor; in this too the right of a married slave woman is half. And similarly, hadd (punishment for disobedience) is penalty and chastisement; this is also half in slaves compared to free persons. In a free person, Allah's favor is complete, so punishment is also complete; in a slave, Allah's favor is deficient, so punishment and hadd are also deficient. 'And the value of his person is diminished' - The value of a slave's life is less than that of a free person due to slavery. So if someone accidentally kills a slave, the killer's 'aqilah (responsible relatives) will owe some less than ten thousand dirhams, even if the slave's price is more than ten thousand. And if the slave's price is less than ten thousand, the price is owed. The proof for diyah being less is that ownership (mālkiyyat) is of two types: (1) Ownership of disposal and possession in property and also ownership of the very thing (ayn). In the free person, this ownership is complete; in the slave it is deficient, because the slave is owner of possession and disposal but not owner of the very thing. (2) Ownership of other than property, such as ownership of usufruct (milk mut'ah) through marriage. This ownership is only acquired by the male, whether free or slave, and the woman cannot own it. In the free man, both types of ownership are complete, so his diyah is also complete. The woman has only the first type of ownership, so her diyah is half that of a man. In the slave, the first type is deficient and the second is complete, so his diyah is slightly less than that of a free man. 'And this is in our view' - The author says that the slave's eligibility for disposal in property is our school. Because the master has given him permission to transact, so his transaction is for himself as principal (aṣālah), not as the master's deputy. Therefore, from his transaction, the primary ruling of transaction, i.e., possession (qabḍ), belongs to him. As for not obtaining ownership of the very thing, i.e., the slave has become owner of possession and disposal but not of the very thing. So ownership of the very thing (milk 'ayn) is something additional to possession (milk yad). That is, the slave has become eligible for transaction and possession, but he did not become owner of it. So milk 'ayn is an additional thing; possession is fundamental, because by taking possession, a person can satisfy his need, even if he did not become owner. And if he became owner but did not obtain possession, he cannot benefit. Anyway, he himself is the owner of disposal and possession; in this the slave is a principal. And milk 'ayn, which is prescribed for reaching possession (milk yad), in that the master is the deputy of the permitted slave. Therefore, ownership is established for the master. In principle, property should also be established for the direct agent (the slave), but since the slave is not eligible for ownership, the master is made his deputy and ownership is established for the master. This passage is actually a response to Imam al-Shafi'i's argument. Imam al-Shafi'i's argument is that a slave is not eligible for disposal and possession, just as he is not eligible for ownership. Hence, the possession he has over his earnings is not his own possession but rather possession by trust (amānah), like a depositary, because if the slave were eligible for disposal and possession, he would also be eligible for ownership, since disposal is a cause of ownership, and the slave is unanimously not eligible for ownership, so he will not be eligible for disposal and possession. The response formulation is that the primary purpose of disposal is possession, and this is obtained by the slave. Possession is the primary purpose because by possession a person can benefit from the thing; there is no need for establishing ownership for that. Therefore, milk 'ayn is not necessary for this primary purpose of possession; rather it is a means to reach possession. So absence of eligibility for a particular means does not necessitate absence of the purpose; rather it is possible that there is another means for the purpose. 'And because of this we made the slave' - Since ownership is not established for the slave, rather the master stands in his place, therefore the slave, in the ruling of ownership and continuation of permission, not in the ruling of disposal, will be like a wakeel (agent). That is, the slave (has) milk yad (possession).
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage from the Urdu commentary on Al-Husami discusses the legal capacity (ahliyyah) of slaves according to the Hanafi school, contrasting it with that of free persons and addressing a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. The passage opens by explaining the concept of dhimma (legal responsibility), which is inherent in humans but weakened in slaves due to slavery (riqq). This deficiency affects several areas: (1) With respect to debts, a slave not permitted to trade cannot be held liable for debts until freed, but if the master grants permission (idhn), the slave's own person and earnings become available for debt satisfaction, allowing the slave to bear debts. (2) In marriage, a slave can marry only two women (half of the free man's capacity), and a slave woman's divorce occurs by two divorces instead of three; her iddah is two menstrual periods instead of three; and her right to division of nights is halved. (3) In criminal penalties (hudud), punishments are halved for slaves compared to free persons, mirroring the reduction in worldly favors. The author then turns to blood money (diyah) for accidental killing: the diyah for a slave is less than that of a free man, determined by a reduced amount (less than 10,000 dirhams) or the slave's actual value if lower. The reasoning is based on ownership powers: a free man has full ownership of both possession/disposal and the very thing; a woman has only the first; a slave has deficient first but complete second, so his diyah is slightly less than a free man's. The passage then shifts to a specific dispute: the Hanafi position that a slave permitted to trade (ma'dhun) can possess and transact, but does not own the thing itself. Imam al-Shafi'i argued that since the slave cannot own, he also cannot possess and transact, because transaction is a cause of ownership. The Hanafi response distinguishes between the purpose of possession (qabd) and ownership (milk ayn): possession is the primary purpose and serves to satisfy need, and it can exist without ownership. The slave's possession is valid because the master's permission makes the slave a principal in possession, while the master acts as deputy for ownership. Thus, the slave is analogous to a wakeel (agent) in matters of possession and continuation of permission, not in the substance of disposal. This commentary emphasizes the Hanafi commitment to istihsan (juristic preference) in allowing the slave's capacity for transaction despite the general incapacity for ownership.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Possession; taking physical or constructive control of property.
ملک عين (milk 'ayn): Ownership of the very thing (the substance/ayn).
أصالة (asalah): Acting as a principal, not as a deputy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i argument that a slave cannot transact because disposal is the cause of ownership?
A) The slave can own with permission, so the argument is false.
B) The primary purpose of disposal is possession, not ownership; possession can exist without ownership.
C) The slave's transaction is invalid but the master can ratify it.
D) The Hanafi school does not dispute Imalam al-Shafi'i on this point.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a ma'dhun slave's transactions by separating possession (primary purpose) from ownership (means), allowing the slave to act as principal in possession while the master is deputy for ownership.

The Grave as Analogous to the Womb: A Hanafi Usul Principle
Analogizing the grave as the first stage of the afterlife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi usul principle that the grave is the first stage of the Hereafter, analogous to the womb for the child, and derive its implications for the rulings of the Hereafter.

Core Idea
The grave is the first stage of the Hereafter; thus the deceased is subject to its rulings as the living are to worldly rulings.

In this passage, the Hanafi tradition moves from worldly rulings to those of the Hereafter by introducing a powerful analogy: the grave is to the Hereafter as the womb is to this world. This analogy establishes that the deceased is not cut off from divine judgment; rather, the grave is the initial stage where reward or punishment begins. This principle underpins many Hanafi rulings on the state of the deceased and the efficacy of actions performed on their behalf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two realms and their first stages
The passage distinguishes between this world (dunya) and the Hereafter (akhirah). Each realm has an initial stage: for dunya, it is the womb/cradle; for akhirah, it is the grave.
2
Recognize the specific analogies
The grave is explicitly compared to the mother's womb for the semen and to the cradle for the child. This is not a generic comparison but a precise analogy.
3
Extract the common element (illah)
Both the womb and the grave serve as the first stage of their respective realms. This common attribute of 'being the first stage' is the effective cause (illah) that links them.
In Hanafi usul, this is an example of qiyas based on shabah (similarity).
4
Apply the analogy to the deceased
Since the grave is the first stage of the Hereafter, the deceased in the grave is in a position analogous to the fetus in the womb – he is in the initial phase of the next life.
5
Derive the implication for ahkam al-akhirah
Just as the living are subject to worldly rulings (duties, rewards, punishments), the deceased in the grave is subject to the rulings of the Hereafter: if righteous, the grave becomes a garden of Paradise; if wicked, a pit of Hell.
This establishes that the deceased continues to experience reward and punishment in the barzakh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما احكام الاخرة : - احکام دنیا کی چار قسمیں بیان کرنے کے بعد احکام آخرت میں میت کا حکم بیان کر رہے ہیں، فرماتے ہیں کہ میت احکام آخرت میں زندوں کے حکم میں ہوتی ہے، اس لئے کہ حکیم آخرت میں میت کے لئے قبر ایسی ہے جیسے مادہ منی کے لئے رحم مادر اور بچہ کے لئے گہوارہ ، پس جیسے رحم مادر اور گہوارہ دنیا کی پہلی منزل ہے، ایسے ہی قبر آخرت کی پہلی منزل ہے، دنیا کے حق میں میت کو قبر میں اس لئے رکھا گیا تا کہ اس پر احکام آخرت جاری ہوں ، پس اگر میت اہل سعادت میں سے ہے، تو قبر اس کے لئے جنت کا باغیچہ ہے، اور اگر اہلِ شقاوت میں سے ہے، تو قبر جہنم کا گڑھا ہے، اللہ تعالیٰ اپنے فضل و کرم سے قبر کو ہمارے لئے جنت کا باغیچہ بنادے، آمین۔ حفظ الرحمن پالن پوری عفی اللہ وعن والد یہ
And as for the rulings of the Hereafter: after explaining the four categories of worldly rulings, they are now explaining the ruling of the deceased in the Hereafter. They say: the deceased, in the rulings of the Hereafter, is in the same position as the living, because the wise one (Allah) has made the grave for the deceased like the mother's womb for the semen and the cradle for the child. Just as the mother's womb and the cradle are the first stage of this world, similarly the grave is the first stage of the Hereafter. The deceased is placed in the grave so that the rulings of the Hereafter may apply to him. So if the deceased is among the people of happiness, the grave becomes a garden of Paradise for him; and if he is among the people of misery, the grave becomes a pit of Hell. May Allah by His grace and generosity make the grave a garden of Paradise for us, Amin. Hifz al-Rahman Palanpuri, may Allah forgive him and his parents.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage transitions from worldly rulings (ahkam al-dunya) to the rulings of the Hereafter (ahkam al-akhirah). The key analogy: the grave is to the Hereafter as the womb is to this world—it is the first stage. Therefore, the deceased is treated as alive for the purposes of punishment or reward in the grave (barzakh). This is not a fiqh ruling but a theological and eschatological principle relevant to usul when considering the status of the dead in legal discussions (e.g., the ability to benefit from actions of the living). The author prays for a good end.

Key Terms
احكام الاخرة (ahkam al-akhirah): Rulings pertaining to the afterlife, especially the state of the deceased in the grave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The mother's womb as the first stage of this world.
C) A temporary resting place until judgment.
D) A garden for the righteous.

Key Takeaway
The grave is the first stage of the Hereafter, analogized to the womb, making the deceased subject to the rulings of reward and punishment.

Effect of Hazl on Mahr: A Hanafi Usul Analysis
Explains rulings of jest in setting mahr amount and type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the effect of hazl (jest) on mahr obligations in marriage and apply the Hanafi usul rule that distinguishes marriage from sale regarding void conditions.

Core Idea
Joking about mahr affects the amount, not marriage validity, because marriage survives void conditions unlike sale.

This passage explains a nuanced Hanafi usul principle: the effect of jest (hazl) on the obligatory dower (mahr). While jesting about the mahr amount or type does not invalidate the marriage, it does alter the financial obligation. Critically, Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes marriage from sale, where jest would void the contract. We will unpack the reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the hazl
Determine what is being jested about in the contract: the quantity of mahr (e.g., 2000 instead of 1000) or the type of mahr (e.g., dinars instead of dirhams).
2
State the ruling on obligation
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, in marriage, only the seriously agreed amount (or the minimum mahr) is obligatory; the jested addition is not owed. For sale, the jested amount becomes the price if the contract is valid.
For hazl in type, if both persist, mahr al-mithl applies.
3
Compare with the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn treat sale and marriage identically: the jested amount does not become obligatory in either. For marriage, the result is the same as Imam Abu Hanifa; for sale, it differs.
The dispute is about sale, not marriage.
4
Identify the 'illa (effective cause) for the distinction
Imam Abu Hanifa argues that marriage is not invalidated by a void condition, whereas sale is. In marriage, seriousness (jidd) and jest (hazl) can coexist; in sale, they cannot.
This is because intention in marriage is more tolerant of irregular terms.
5
Apply the distinction to hazl in type
When the type of mahr is jested (e.g., dinars instead of dirhams), the actual mahr was not mentioned, so the marriage is without a fixed dower. Hence, mahr al-mithl is obligatory unless both parties agree to abandon the jest.
In sale, such jest voids the contract because the type cannot be reconciled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یہاں امام صاحب اور صاحبین سب کے نزدیک اقل مہر یعنی ایک ہزار واجب ہوگا، دو ہزار واجب نہ ہوگا ، صاحبین کے نزدیک تو بیع اور نکاح کا مسئلہ ایک ہی ہے البتہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک بیچ اور نکاح میں فرق ہے، بیچ میں ہنرل کی صورت میں دو ہزار ثمن واجب ہوتا ہے، اور نکاح میں ہنرل کی صورت میں ایک ہزار واجب ہوگا، وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ بیچ میں اگر دو ہزار کوشن نہ قرار دیا جائے ، بلکہ ایک ہزار کوشن قرار دیا جائے تو دوسرے ایک ہزار کو قبول کرنے کی شرط فاسد ہوگی ، اور شرط فاسد سے بیع فاسد ہو جاتی ہے، اور نکاح شرط فاسد سے فاسد نہیں ہوتا ہے، لہذا دو ہزار جس کا دونوں نے ہنرل کیا ہے باطل نہ ہوگا، کیونکہ یہاں موافقت فی الجد یعنی ایک ہزار پر نکاح ہونا اور موافقت فی البزل یعنی دو ہزار کو صرف مذا قأذکر کرنا دونوں جمع ہو سکتے ہیں، لہذا دو ہزار میں ایک ہزار تو سنجیدگی سے کہا ہے، وہ مہر بنے گا ، اور دوسرا ایک ہزار جو مذاقا کہا ہے وہ واجب نہ ہوگا، تو یہ مثال ہوئی مقدار مہر میں ہنرل کی ، اور اگر میاں بیوی نے جنس مہر میں ہنرل کیا جیسے اصل مہر تو دو ہزار درہم ہیں مگر لوگوں کے سامنے انہوں نے دو ہزار دینار کا ذکر کیا، اب اگر دونوں ہنرل پر برقرار ہوں، یا خالی الذھن ہوں، یا ہنرل میں دونوں کا اختلاف ہو جائے تو ان سب صورتوں میں مہر مثل واجب ہوتا ہے، کیونکہ جو چیز اصل مہر ہے اس کا نکاح میں ذکر ہی نہیں آیا تو گویا بغیر مہر کے نکاح ہوا، اور ایسی صورت میں مہر مثل واجب ہوتا ہے، اور اگر ہنرل سے اعراض پر دونوں کا اتفاق ہو جائے تو مسمی واجب ہوگا، برخلاف بیع کے، کیونکہ بیع بغیر شمن کے صحیح ہی نہیں ہوتی ہے، بلکہ بیع فاسد ہو جاتی ہے، پس جنس ثمن میں ہنرل پر موافقت اور اصل بیچ میں جد پر موافقت دونوں میں تطبیق نہیں ہوسکتی ہے، کیونکہ ہنرل کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ وہ ثمن نہ ہو اور اصل بیع میں جد کا تقاضا ہے کہ وہ شمن ہو، تو تعارض ہو گیا، لہذا بیع فاسد ہو جائے گی، اور نکاح میں دونوں موافقتوں میں تطبیق ممکن ہے جیسا کہ او پر مذکور ہوا۔
Here, according to Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the minimum dower (mahr) of one thousand is obligatory, not two thousand. For the two companions, the ruling of sale and marriage is the same, but for Imam Abu Hanifa, there is a difference between sale and marriage. In sale, with jest, two thousand as price becomes obligatory, while in marriage, with jest, one thousand becomes obligatory. The reason for the difference is that in sale, if two thousand is not set as the price, but one thousand is set as the price, then the condition to accept the other one thousand is void, and a void condition makes the sale void. However, marriage is not invalidated by a void condition. Therefore, the two thousand which they jested about is not void, because here agreement in seriousness (marriage on one thousand) and agreement in jest (mentioning two thousand only as a joke) can both be combined. So of the two thousand, one thousand was said seriously, that becomes the dower, and the other one thousand said jokingly is not obligatory. This is an example of hazl in the quantity of mahr. If the spouses jest in the type of mahr, e.g., the actual mahr is two thousand dirhams, but in front of people they mention two thousand dinars, then if both persist on the jest, or are free-minded, or they differ about the jest, in all these cases mahr al-mithl (proper dower) is obligatory, because the actual mahr was not mentioned in the marriage, so it is as if the marriage was without dower, and in such case mahr al-mithl is obligatory. If they agree to abandon the jest, then the named (musamma) dower is obligatory, unlike sale, because sale is not valid without a price; rather, it becomes void. So in the type of price, agreement on jest and agreement on seriousness in the actual sale cannot be reconciled, because jest requires that it not be the price, while seriousness requires that it be the price; thus there is contradiction, so the sale becomes void. In marriage, reconciliation between the two agreements is possible, as mentioned above.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the ruling of hazl (jest) in the context of marriage (nikah) and sale (bai'). It opens by stating the basic ruling: when hazl occurs in the quantity of mahr, the minimum dower (one thousand dirhams according to Hanafi law) is obligatory, not the higher amount jested about. The text then distinguishes between the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). For the Sahibayn, sale and marriage are treated identically in this regard, while for Imam Abu Hanifa, there is a difference. The reason is that in sale, a void condition (like a jested price) renders the entire sale void, whereas in marriage, a void condition does not invalidate the marriage itself. Following the author's order, the commentator then explains that when hazl is in the type (jins) of mahr, if the actual mahr is not mentioned, the marriage is considered without a fixed dower, so mahr al-mithl is obligatory unless both parties agree to abandon the jest. This contrasts with sale, where a jest about the type of price makes the sale void because the contradiction between seriousness and jest cannot be resolved. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh, specifically the rulings on mahr and sale.

Key Terms
ہنرل (ہزل) (hazl): Jest or joke in a legal contract, specifically in stating the dower or price.
مہر (mahr): The dower or bridal gift obligatory upon marriage.
مہر مثل (mahr al-mithl): Proper dower: the amount appropriate for a woman in her social position, used when no specific mahr is fixed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if spouses jest about the mahr amount (saying 2000 but agreeing on 1000), what is the ruling?
A) 2000 dirhams is obligatory because the jest is disregarded
B) 1000 dirhams is obligatory because that is the serious agreement
C) 1500 dirhams as an average
D) Mahr al-mithl (proper dower) because the jest invalidates the entire dower

Key Takeaway
Jesting about mahr does not invalidate marriage; only the serious portion is binding, and the nature of marriage allows reconciliation of jest and seriousness unlike sale.

Safar as a Cause of Concession: Ruling on Breaking a Fast During Travel
Explains the rulings of travel on salah and fasting with a focus on kaffara for a traveler who breaks a fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a traveler who starts a Ramadan fast and then breaks it due to travel is sinful but only owes qada', and explain the role of doubt (shubha) in preventing kaffara.

Core Idea
Travel permits concessions, but a started fast is binding; breaking is sinful, yet the presence of a permissible cause creates a doubt that bars kaffara.

In this passage, we explore how travel (safar) functions as a cause of legal concession in ibadat. While travel clearly permits shortening prayer and postponing fasting, a nuanced issue arises when a traveler starts a fast and then breaks it. The commentary contrasts travel with illness, illustrating how the voluntary nature of travel and the absence of compulsion affect the obligation of kaffara. This lesson unpacks the reasoning: once a fast begins, it becomes binding, but the mere existence of a permissible cause (travel) creates a doubt that saves the violator from kaffara, though not from sin and qada'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Recognize that fasting in Ramadan is fard. Starting a fast makes it binding (lazim). Travel is a cause that permits breaking the fast before starting, but once started, the obligation remains.
The traveler is not compelled to break, unlike a sick person.
2
Compare travel and illness
Note the distinction: travel is voluntary, illness involuntary. Illness compels breaking, travel does not. Hence, if a sick person breaks, no sin or kaffara; if a traveler breaks, sin but only qada' due to doubt.
The doubt (shubha) arises from the existence of a cause that permits breaking.
3
Trace the legal effect of the cause
The cause (safar) is present at the time of breaking. This presence creates a shubha that prevents kaffara, even though breaking is still sinful. This is a Hanafi principle: any doubt in the obligation of a penalty drops it.
Contrast: if no cause (e.g., resident breaks), kaffara is obligatory.
4
Apply the same reasoning to illness after breaking
If a healthy person breaks and then becomes ill, no kaffara because illness is involuntary and the person is now akin to a sick person who broke – illness itself does not obligate kaffara.
This shows the ruling is linked to the state at the time of breaking, not afterward.
5
Derive the usul principle
Conclude: the presence of a valid cause of permission at the time of the prohibited act creates a doubt that negates the penalty (kaffara), though the act itself remains sinful and requires qada'. This is a specific application of the broader usul maxim 'al-shubha tudfa' al-hadd' (doubt averts the prescribed punishment).
This principle is not universal across all schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما السفر : - عوارض مکتبہ میں سے چھٹا عارض سفر ہے ، جو تخفیف کے اسباب میں سے ہے، اور سفر کی وجہ سے چار رکعت والی نمازوں میں قصر ہو گا یعنی دورکعت پڑھے گا، اور روزہ میں بھی تاخیر کی اجازت ہوگی ، اور مرض میں بھی روزے کی تاخیر کی اجازت ہوتی ہے، مگر مرض اور سفر میں فرق ہے، سفر اختیاری امور میں سے ہے، اور مرض بندے کے اختیار کی چیز نہیں ہے، نیز سفر ایسی ضرورت کو واجب نہیں کرنا جو داعی الی الافطار ہو، یعنی مسافر روزہ توڑنے پر مجبور ہو جائے ایسا نہیں ہے بلکہ بغیر مشقت کے روزہ رکھنے پر قادر ہوتا ہے، بلکہ سفر کو ترک کر کے ضرورت پوری کر سکتا ہے اور مرض ایسی ضرورت کو واجب کرتا ہے جو داعی الی الافطار ہو یعنی مریض روزہ توڑنے پر مجبور ہو جاتا ہے، پس جب مرض اور سفر میں فرق ہے تو جو شخص مسافر ہو اور صبح کو اس نے روزہ کی نیت کی ، یا مقیم ہے اور روزہ کی نیت کر لی، پھر اس نے سفر شروع کیا، تو دونوں صورتوں میں افطار ( روزہ توڑنا ) درست نہ ہوگا، کیونکہ روزہ شروع کرنے سے لازم ہو گیا، اور مسافر روزہ پر قادر بھی ہے، کوئی ایسی لازمی ضرورت بھی نہیں جو داعی الی الافطار ہو، لہذا اگر افطار کرے گا تو گنہگار ہوگا، برخلاف مریض کے کہ اس نے اگر تکلیف روزہ رکھا، پھر تو ڑ دیا تو اس پر کفارہ نہیں ہے۔ لیکن چونکہ نفس سفر جو صحیح افطار ہے وہ موجود ہے تو اس شبہ کی وجہ سے اگر وہ روزہ توڑ دے گا تو کفارہ واجب نہ ہوگا ،صرف قضا لازم ہوگی لیکن اگر مقیم نے بحالت اقامت روزہ توڑ دیا اور پھر سفر شروع کیا تو کفارہ واجب ہوگا، کیونکہ یہاں شبہ نہیں ہے، اس لئے کہ سفر جو افطار کو مباح کرنے والا ہے وہ روزہ توڑنے کے بعد پایا گیا، اس کے برخلاف اگر تندرست صائم نے روزہ توڑ دیا، پھر بیمار ہوا تو اس پر کفارہ واجب نہ ہوگا ، کیونکہ مرض بندے کی اختیاری چیز نہیں ہے، بلکہ امر سماوی ہے، تو یہ ایسا ہو گیا کہ گویا اس نے بیماری کی حالت میں افطار کیا، اور بیماری کی حالت میں افطار کرنے سے کفارہ واجب نہیں ہوتا ہے۔
And as for travel: The sixth impediment among the written impediments is travel, which is among the causes of lightening (takhfif). Due to travel, shortening (qasr) will be done in the four-rak'at prayers – i.e., he will pray two rak'ahs – and postponement of fasting is also permitted. Illness also permits postponement of fasting, but there is a difference between illness and travel: travel is among voluntary matters, while illness is not under the control of the servant. Also, travel does not necessitate a need that compels breaking the fast – i.e., a traveler is not forced to break his fast; rather, he is able to fast without hardship. In fact, he can fulfill his need by abandoning travel. Illness necessitates a need that compels breaking the fast – i.e., a sick person is forced to break his fast. Therefore, when there is a difference between illness and travel, if a person is a traveler and intends to fast in the morning, or is a resident and intends to fast, then commences travel, in both cases breaking the fast is not valid, because fasting has become obligatory upon starting it, and the traveler is able to fast, and there is no necessary need that compels breaking. So if he breaks, he will be sinful, contrary to the sick person: if he endured fasting and then broke it, there is no kaffara upon him. However, since the travel itself which permits valid breaking exists, due to this doubt, if he breaks his fast, kaffara will not be obligatory; only qada' is necessary. But if a resident breaks his fast while still a resident and then starts travel, kaffara is obligatory, because there is no doubt here since the travel which permits breaking was found after breaking. On the other hand, if a healthy fasting person breaks his fast and then becomes sick, no kaffara is obligatory, because illness is not a voluntary matter but a divine decree; it is as if he broke his fast while sick, and breaking while sick does not necessitate kaffara.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage discusses travel (safar) as one of the 'awariḍ that cause concessions in ibadat. The author first states that travel allows qasr of salah (shortening four-rak'at prayers to two) and postponement of fasting. He contrasts travel with illness: travel is voluntary, illness involuntary. A traveler is generally able to fast without hardship, so if he starts a fast and then travels, he must complete it; breaking it would be sinful. However, due to the inherent permission of breaking in travel, if he breaks, only qada' is required, not kaffara. If he breaks before traveling while still a resident, kaffara is obligatory. For illness, if a sick person breaks after starting a fast, no kaffara because illness is not voluntary and compels breaking. The key principle is that once a fast is validly started, it becomes binding; only if there is a valid excuse (like illness from the start) can it be broken without sin, but due to the nature of travel, the presence of the cause of permission creates doubt that prevents kaffara.

Key Terms
سفر (safar): Travel, defined as a journey that permits concessions such as qasr of salah and breaking fast in Ramadan.
قصر (qasr): Shortening of the four-rak'at prayers to two rak'ahs during travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a traveler starts a fast in the morning and then breaks it due to the difficulty of travel, what is the required expiation according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Kaffara and qada'
B) Only qada'
C) Neither qada' nor kaffara
D) Only kaffara

Key Takeaway
Travel is a cause of concession, but once a fast is started, breaking it is sinful; however, due to the doubt created by the existence of the permissible cause, only qada' is required, not kaffara.

Definition and Types of Duress (Ikrah) in Hanafi Usul
Defines the two types of duress and their effects on choice and consent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ikrah kamil and ikrah qasir and state their effects on choice and consent.

Core Idea
Complete duress destroys choice; incomplete duress only removes consent.

In Hanafi usul, duress (ikrah) is a key concept that affects the validity of actions. This passage from Al-Husami defines two types: complete and incomplete. Understanding this distinction is foundational for applying rulings related to coerced acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
The passage explicitly states that ikrah is of two types: kamil (complete) and qasir (incomplete).
2
Understand the effect of complete duress
Complete duress corrupts choice (ikhtiyar) and necessitates compulsion (ilja'), meaning the person is forced to act.
3
Understand the effect of incomplete duress
Incomplete duress removes consent (rida) but does not necessitate compulsion.
4
Note the impact on legal capacity
Duress does not contradict legal capacity (ahliyyah) nor does it cause removal of the address (khitab); the person remains legally addressed.
The passage emphasizes that the one under duress is still tested (mubtala) and the address applies.

الْقَاصِرُ شُبُهَةً فِي دَرهِ الْحَدِ عَنْهَا دُونَ الرَّجْلِ فَثَبَتَ بِهَذِهِ الْجُمْلَةِ أَنَّ الْإِكْرَاهَ لَا يَصْلَحُ لِإِبْطَالِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْأَقْوَالِ وَالْأَفْعَالِ جُمْلَةً إِلَّا بِدَلِيْلٍ غَيَّرَهُ عَلَى مِثَالِ فِعْلِ الطَّائِعِ وَإِنَّمَا يَظْهَرُ أَثَرُ الْإِكْرَامِ إِذَا تَكَامَلَ فِى تَبْدِيلِ النِّسْبَةِ وَأَثَرُهُ إِذَا قَصَرَ فِي تَفُوِيْتِ الرّضَاءِ فَيَفْسُدُ قَامَتْ دَلَالَةٌ عَدَمِهِ وَإِذَا اِتَّصَلَ الْإِكْرَاهُ بِقَبُوْلِ الْمَالِ فِى الْخُلْعِ فَإِنَّ الطَّلَاقَ يَقَعُ وَالْمَالُ لَا يَجِبْ لِاَنَّ الْإِكْرَاهَ يَعْدِمُ الرِضَاءَ بِالسَّبَبِ وَالْحُكْمِ جَمِيْعًا وَالْمَالُ يَنْعَدِمُ عِنْدَ عَدَمِ الرَّضَاءِ فَكَانَ الْمَالَ لَمْ يُوْجَدُ فَوَقَعَ الطَّلَاقُ بِغَيْرِ مَالٍ كَطَلَاقِ الصَّغِيرَةِ عَلَى مَالٍ بِخِلَافِ الْهَزْلِ لِأَنَّهُ دوسرا قاصر جو رضا کو معدوم کرتا ہے اور الجاء کو ثابت نہیں کرتا ہے، اور اکراہ اپنی تمام قسموں کے ساتھ اہلیت کے منافی نہیں ہے، اور نہ کسی حال میں خطاب کے سقوط کو ثابت کرتا ہے، اس لئے کہ مگر متلی ہے، اور ابتلاء خطاب کو ثابت کرتا ہے، کیا تو نہیں دیکھتا ہے کہ مگرہ متردد ہے فرض، حرام، اباحت اور رخصت کے درمیان ، اور اس میں مکرہ کبھی گنہ گار ہوتا ہے اور کبھی ماجور ہوتا ہے، لہذا قتل، زخم اور زنا میں اکراہ کے عذر کی وجہ سے بالکل رخصت نہیں ہے، اور
And as for duress (ikrah), it is of two types: complete (kamil), which corrupts choice and necessitates compulsion (ilja'), and incomplete (qasir), which removes consent (rida) but does not necessitate compulsion. Duress in its entirety does not contradict legal capacity (ahliyyah) nor does it cause the removal of address (khitab) in any situation, because the one under duress is subject to trial (mubtala), and trial establishes the address. Do you not see that he is torn between obligation, prohibition, permissibility, and concession? Sometimes he sins, sometimes he is rewarded. Therefore, there is no concession in killing, wounding, and zina under the excuse of duress at all. And there is no prohibition with complete duress in [eating] carrion, wine, and pork. And concession is granted in uttering words of disbelief, spoiling prayer and fasting, destroying another's property, violating ihram, and a woman permitting zina under complete duress. Her act differs from his act in concession because the lineage of the child is not cut off from her, so she is not analogous to killing, unlike the man. For this reason, incomplete duress creates a doubt that prevents hadd from her but not from the man. Thus, it is established from this summary that duress is not suitable to invalidate any words or actions entirely except by a specific proof (dalil) that changes it [to be] like the act of a willing person. The effect of complete duress appears in changing the attribution (nisba), and its effect when incomplete appears in removing consent. So by duress, what is subject to annulment and depends on consent becomes invalid, such as sale and lease. All acknowledgments (iqrar) are not valid because their validity depends on the establishment of the thing acknowledged, and there is evidence of its non-existence. And when duress is attached to the acceptance of compensation in khul', then divorce occurs but the compensation is not obligatory, because duress removes consent for both the cause and the ruling, and compensation is absent when consent is absent. So it is as if the compensation was not given, and divorce occurs without compensation, like the divorce of a minor girl with compensation, unlike jest (hazl), because it prevents consent for the ruling but not the cause, so it is like a condition of option (khiyar) as already mentioned. Translation: And in any case, duress is of two types, one complete which corrupts choice and establishes compulsion, and the other incomplete which removes consent and does not establish compulsion. And duress in all its types is not opposed to capacity, nor does it cause the fall of address in any situation, because the person under duress is tried, and trial establishes the address. Do you not see that the person under duress is torn between obligation, prohibition, permission, and concession? And in it, the person under duress sometimes sins and sometimes is rewarded. Therefore, there is no concession in killing, wounding, and zina under the excuse of duress at all, and...
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed discussion of duress (ikrah) in Hanafi usul. The author defines two types: complete duress (ikrah kamil), which destroys free choice and forces action, and incomplete duress (ikrah qasir), which removes consent but does not force the action. Despite duress, the person retains legal capacity (ahliyyah) and is still addressed by the law (khitab). The author then lists acts for which there is no concession under duress: killing, wounding, and zina. For other acts like eating carrion, drinking wine, under complete duress, there is no prohibition. Concession is granted for uttering words of disbelief, spoiling prayer/fast, destroying property, violating ihram, and a woman allowing zina under complete duress. The difference between man and woman in zina is because lineage is attached to the woman. Incomplete duress creates a doubt that prevents hadd from the woman but not the man. The passage then states that duress does not invalidate acts or words without a specific proof. Complete duress affects the attribution of the act, while incomplete duress affects consent. Consequently, contracts requiring consent (sale, lease) are invalid under duress, and acknowledgments (iqrar) are invalid because the truth of the statement is contradicted by evidence. In khul', if duress is attached to acceptance of compensation, divorce occurs but compensation is not due because consent is lacking. This differs from jest (hazl), which only removes consent for the ruling not the cause.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Duress; compelling a person to do something against his will. Two types: kamil (complete) and qasir (incomplete).
إلجاء (ilja'): Compulsion; being forced to act, which removes choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is true about ikrah kamil according to the passage?
A) It removes consent but not choice.
B) It corrupts choice and necessitates compulsion.
C) It removes legal capacity.
D) It permits killing.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes two types of duress: complete duress destroys choice, incomplete duress only removes consent.

intermediate
8 min
Legal Effects of Duress on Actions and Contracts
Lists acts permitted and prohibited under duress, and effect on contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which acts are permitted under complete duress and which are not, and explain the effect of duress on contracts like sale and khul'.

Core Idea
Duress does not permit killing or zina, but permits eating carrion; complete duress can void contracts but not divorce in khul' without compensation.

This passage applies the definition of duress to specific actions. It lists acts that are never permitted even under duress (killing, wounding, zina), acts that become permitted under complete duress (eating carrion, uttering disbelief), and explains the effect on contracts. Understanding these applications is vital for applying usul to fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify acts with no concession under duress
The passage firmly states that killing, wounding, and zina have no concession (rukhsa) under duress.
2
Identify acts with concession under complete duress
Eating carrion, wine, pork; uttering disbelief; spoiling prayer/fast; destroying property; violating ihram; woman permitting zina are all permitted as concessions under ikrah kamil.
3
Note the difference between man and woman in zina
For a woman, the lineage of the child is not cut off, so she is not analogous to killing; for a man, no such consideration.
Incomplete duress creates a doubt that prevents hadd for the woman but not the man.
4
Understand effect on contracts and acknowledgments
Contracts depending on consent (sale, lease) become invalid under duress; acknowledgments (iqrar) are invalid because evidence disproves the claim.
5
Apply to khul' and distinguish from jest
In khul', if duress is on acceptance of compensation, divorce occurs but compensation is not due. In jest, divorce with compensation is valid because consent is only lacking for the ruling, not the cause.

And as for duress (ikrah), it is of two types: complete (kamil), which corrupts choice and necessitates compulsion (ilja'), and incomplete (qasir), which removes consent (rida) but does not necessitate compulsion. Duress in its entirety does not contradict legal capacity (ahliyyah) nor does it cause the removal of address (khitab) in any situation, because the one under duress is subject to trial (mubtala), and trial establishes the address. Commentary
This passage provides a detailed discussion of duress (ikrah) in Hanafi usul. The author defines two types: complete duress (ikrah kamil), which destroys free choice and forces action, and incomplete duress (ikrah qasir), which removes consent but does not force the action. Despite duress, the person retains legal capacity (ahliyyah) and is still addressed by the law (khitab). The author then lists acts for which there is no concession under duress: killing, wounding, and zina. For other acts like eating carrion, drinking wine, under complete duress, there is no prohibition. Concession is granted for uttering words of disbelief, spoiling prayer/fast, destroying property, violating ihram, and a woman allowing zina under complete duress. The difference between man and woman in zina is because lineage is attached to the woman. Incomplete duress creates a doubt that prevents hadd from the woman but not the man. The passage then states that duress does not invalidate acts or words without a specific proof. Complete duress affects the attribution of the act, while incomplete duress affects consent. Consequently, contracts requiring consent (sale, lease) are invalid under duress, and acknowledgments (iqrar) are invalid because the truth of the statement is contradicted by evidence. In khul', if duress is attached to acceptance of compensation, divorce occurs but compensation is not due because consent is lacking. This differs from jest (hazl), which only removes consent for the ruling not the cause.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Duress; compelling a person to do something against his will. Two types: kamil (complete) and qasir (incomplete).
إلجاء (ilja'): Compulsion; being forced to act, which removes choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Eating carrion
B) Uttering words of disbelief
C) Killing an innocent person
D) Destroying another's property

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi law, duress does not permit killing, wounding, or zina; complete duress permits other acts like eating carrion; contracts requiring consent are invalid.

Gender-Based Rukhsat in Zina under Duress and Its Effect on Hadd
Explains why female gets rukhsat for zina under duress but male does not, and implications for incomplete duress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that the effect of duress on hadd for zina differs by gender, deriving the ruling from the qiyas khafi of lineage attribution.

Core Idea
For men, zina under duress severs lineage like killing a child; for women it does not—hence duress grants shubha only for women.

In this passage, the author addresses a critical question in Hanafi usul: why does a woman receive a concession (rukhsat) for committing zina under complete duress, while a man does not? The answer lies in a subtle analogy (qiyas khafi) based on lineage attribution. Understanding this distinction is essential for applying the rules of duress to the fixed punishment (hadd) for zina.

Guided Path
1
Identify the implied question
Recognize that the passage begins by answering an unstated objection: if complete duress gives women a concession, why not men? This sets up the need for a distinguishing factor.
The implied question is a standard rhetorical device in Hanafi manuals.
2
Extract the operative analogy (qiyas)
Find the analogy: For men, zina is likened to killing a child because it severs paternal lineage (nasab). For women, lineage is never severed because the child is always attributed to her.
This is a qiyas khafi (subtle analogy) based on the 'illa of lineage severance.
3
Apply the analogy to complete duress
Under complete duress, the woman's act is not analogous to killing a child, so she gets a concession. The man's act is analogous to killing a child (forbidden under any duress), so he gets no concession.
Complete duress (ikrāh kāmil) removes consent and choice, but the analogy overrides the concession for men.
4
Extend the ruling to incomplete duress
Since complete duress grants a concession to women, incomplete duress (ikrāh qāṣir) creates a shubha (doubt) that prevents hadd for women. For men, no concession exists in complete duress, so no shubha arises from incomplete duress, and hadd applies.
Shubha is a broader concept in Hanafi law that can avert hadd.
5
State the final rule
Formulate the rule: Under incomplete duress, a woman is exempt from hadd for zina; a man is liable to hadd. Under complete duress, both are exempt (for the man due to shubha from the duress itself).
The passage specifies that complete duress creates shubha even for the man, so hadd is dropped for both in that case.

ہوئے ان سب امور کو نہ کیا اور اس کی وجہ سے اس کی جان چلی گئی تو وہ شھید ہوگا۔ وانما فارق فعلها - سوال مقدر جواب ہے، جس کی تقریر یہ ہے کہ اکراہ کامل میں جس طرح عورت کو رخصت دی گئی زنا پر قدرت دینے کی تو یہ رخصت مرد کو بھی حاصل ہونی چاہیے، حالانکہ مرد کو رخصت نہیں ہے۔ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ عورت کا فعل زنا مرد کے فعل زنا سے الگ ہے، کیونکہ عورت سے ولد کی نسبت کسی بھی حال میں منقطع نہیں ہوتی ہے، حتی کہ زنا کی صورت میں بھی بچہ زانیہ کی طرف ہی منسوب ہوتا ہے ، مرد کی طرف منسوب نہیں ہوتا، اس کے برخلاف زانی مرد سے بچہ کی نسبت منقطع ہو جاتی ہے، تو مرد کے حق میں زنا قتل ولد کے حکم میں ہوگا ، اور عورت کے حق میں زنا قتل ولد کے حکم میں نہ ہوگا، لہذا مرد کو رخصت نہ ہوگی ،عورت کو رخصت ملے گی۔ ولهذا اوجب الاكراه القاصر :- جب اکراہ کامل عورت کے حق میں رخصت ثابت کرتا ہے، اور مرد کے حق میں رخصت ثابت نہیں کرتا تو اکراہ قاصر عورت سے حد دفع کرنے میں تو شبہ ثابت کرے گا، لیکن مرد سے حد دفع کرنے میں شبہ ثابت نہیں کرے گا، لہذا اگر عورت اکراہ قاصر کے ساتھ زنا پر مجبور کی گئی تو اس پر زنا کی حد نہ لگے گی ، کیونکہ اکراہ کامل میں عورت کو رخصت ملتی ہے تو اکراہ قاصر سے شہر رخصت پیدا ہوگا اور شبہ سے حد ساقط ہو جاتی ہے، اور مرد کو چونکہ اکراہ کامل کی صورت میں رخصت نہیں ملتی تو اکراہ قاصر سے اس کے حق میں شبہ بھی پیدا نہ ہوگا، لہذا اکراہ قاصر کی صورت میں زنا سے مرد پر حد لگے گی ، ہاں اکراہ کامل میں مرد کے حق میں بھی شبہ ثابت ہوگا اور حد نہ لگے گی ۔
He did not do any of these things and because of that his life was lost, then he will be a martyr. And why the act differs - it is the answer to an implied question, the exposition of which is that just as in complete duress a concession was given to the woman to have power over zina, this concession should also be obtained by the man, while the man does not have a concession. The answer is that the woman's act of zina is different from the man's act of zina, because the attribution of a child to a woman is never cut off in any situation, even in the case of zina the child is attributed to the adulteress, not attributed to the man; on the contrary, from the adulterous man the attribution of the child is cut off. So for the man, zina is in the ruling of killing a child, and for the woman, zina is not in the ruling of killing a child. Hence the man will not have a concession, the woman will get a concession. And for this reason, incomplete duress necessitates: When complete duress establishes a concession for the woman, and does not establish a concession for the man, then incomplete duress will establish shubha (doubt) for the woman in repelling the hadd, but will not establish shubha for the man in repelling the hadd. Therefore, if a woman is compelled to zina with incomplete duress, the hadd of zina will not be imposed on her, because in complete duress the woman gets a concession, so from incomplete duress a kind of concession arises, and the hadd is dropped due to shubha. And since the man does not get a concession in the case of complete duress, no shubha will arise for him from incomplete duress. Hence in the case of incomplete duress, the hadd will be imposed on the man for zina. Yes, in complete duress, shubha will be established for the man as well, and the hadd will not be imposed.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning explaining the gender-based difference in the effect of duress on zina and the subsequent impact of incomplete duress on hadd. The author addresses an implied question: if complete duress grants a concession to women for zina, why not to men? The answer is founded on lineage attribution: a child is always attributed to the mother, even in adultery, so for her, zina does not equate to killing a child; for the man, zina severs lineage and thus resembles killing a child. Hence the woman gets a concession (rukhsat) under complete duress, while the man does not. Building on this, the author explains that incomplete duress (ikrah qasir) creates a shubha (doubt) that prevents hadd for women, but not for men, because the concession in complete duress for women lowers the threshold for shubha in incomplete duress. For men, since no concession exists in complete duress, incomplete duress does not create shubha; therefore hadd is imposed on men under incomplete duress, but women are exempt. This is a Hanafi position reflecting the interplay of duress, shubha, and hadd.

Key Terms
اکراہ کامل (ikrāh kāmil): Complete duress that threatens life or severe harm, removing choice (ikhtiyār) and consent (riḍā).
اکراہ قاصر (ikrāh qāṣir): Incomplete duress that removes consent but not choice, e.g., threats of beating or imprisonment.
حد (ḥadd): Fixed corporal punishment prescribed for specific offenses like zina.
رخصت (rukḥṣat): Concession or permission granted due to necessity or duress.
شبہ (shubhah): Doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi usul, the effect of duress on hadd for zina is gender-specific because lineage attribution creates a qiyas khafi that grants women a concession and shubha under incomplete duress, while men remain liable.

Duress Does Not Invalidate Statements and Actions Unless a Modifier Exists
States that duress does not negate eligibility or address; actions and statements remain valid unless a modifier intervenes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that duress does not negate legal eligibility or address, and apply it to distinguish between valid and invalid coerced statements and actions based on the presence of a modifying factor.

Core Idea
Duress alone does not nullify acts; only an external modifier (mujir) changes the ruling.

This passage establishes a foundational principle: duress (ikrah) does not strip a person of legal eligibility (ahliyyah) nor does it suspend the legal address (khitab). Consequently, statements (e.g., divorce, emancipation) and actions (e.g., killing, property damage) made under duress are generally valid and carry their usual rulings. However, if a modifying factor (mujir) or evidence (dalil) intervenes, the ruling may change—just as with a non-coerced person. This principle is central to understanding Hanafi usul al-fiqh on compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
The passage asserts that duress does not invalidate statements or actions by itself. The coerced person retains legal capacity and accountability.
Think of the coerced person's act as presumptively valid.
2
Understand the Basis: Ahliyyah and Khitab
Ahliyyah (legal eligibility) is not negated by duress; only insanity or minority can remove it. Similarly, the address (khitab) of the Law remains directed at the coerced person.
The coerced person is still obligated, though consent is absent.
3
Recognize the Role of the Modifier (Mujir)
A modifer—like a condition (ta'liq), exception (istithna'), or contextual factor (dar al-harb, shubha)—can override the default validity. When a modifier exists, the act becomes invalid.
The modifier is external; it doesn't arise from duress itself.
4
Apply the Parallel with Non-Coerced Persons
The same rule applies to both coerced and non-coerced: acts are valid unless a modifier intervenes. For example, a divorce with a condition ('if you enter the house') does not take effect.
This symmetry reinforces that duress does not change the basic mechanism.
5
Work Through the Examples Given
Study the examples: coerced divorce without modifier is valid; coerced drinking of wine is punishable unless a modifier like dar al-harb or shubha is present. This illustrates how the principle operates.
Focus on the contrast between presence and absence of a modifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مصنف عالی فرماتے ہیں کہ ماسبق کی تقریر سے کہ اکراہ اہلیت کے منافی نہیں ہے، اور نہ مکرہ سے خطاب ساقط ہوتا ہے، یہ بات ثابت ہوگئی کہ اکراہ نہ تو اقوال مثل طلاق ، عتاق وغیرہ کو باطل کرنے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے اور نہ افعال یعنی قتل، اتلاف مال غیر وغیرہ کو باطل کرنے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے، ہاں اگر کوئی ایسی دلیل پائی جائے جو اس کے قول وفعل کو بدل دے تو اس وقت اس کا قول وفعل باطل ہو جائے گا جیسے غیر مکرہ کا بھی یہی حکم ہے کہ اس کا قول و فعل صحیح ہے، اور اگر اس کو بدلنے والی کوئی دلیل پائی جائے تو اس کا قول و فعل باطل ہو جاتا ہے، جیسے کوئی اپنی بیوی سے کہے انت طالق تو فورا طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی لیکن اگر کوئی مغیر لاحق ہو جائے جیسے تعلیق یا استثناء تو طلاق واقع نہ ہوگی، جیسے انت طالق کے بعد ان شاء الله کہہ دیا یا ان دخلت الدار کہہ دیا، ایسے ہی اگر کسی نے شراب پی یا زنا کیا تو اس کا یہ فعل معتبر ہے، اس پر حد جاری ہوگی لیکن اگر کوئی مغیر یا مانع پایا جائے جیسے یہ افعال دار الحرب میں پائے گئے ، یا ان کے ثبوت میں شبہ پیدا ہو گیا، تو یہ افعال معتبر نہ ہوں گے، یہی حکم مکرہ کا ہے کہ اس کے اقوال وافعال معتبر ہیں لیکن اگر مغیر پایا جائے تو اس کے اقوال و افعال معتبر اور صحیح نہ ہوں گے، جیسے آگے بیان کریں گے کہ بعض صورتوں میں قول و فعل مگرہ سے باطل ہو کر مگر ہ کی طرف منسوب ہوتا ہے۔
The eminent author says: From the previous exposition that duress is not contrary to eligibility, nor is the address (khitab) dropped from the coerced person, it became established that duress neither has the capacity to invalidate statements like divorce, emancipation etc., nor does it have the capacity to invalidate actions like killing, destroying the property of others etc. Yes, if any evidence is found that changes his statement and action, then at that time his statement and action will become invalid, just as the ruling for the non-coerced person is also that his statement and action are valid, and if evidence that changes it is found, then his statement and action become invalid. For example, if someone says to his wife "You are divorced" (anti taliq), then divorce occurs immediately, but if a modifier (mujir) attaches such as a condition (ta'liq) or exception (istithna'), then divorce will not occur, like saying after "anti taliq" [?] "insha' Allah" or "if you enter the house." Similarly, if someone drinks wine or commits zina, his action is valid; hadd will be imposed on him, but if a modifier or preventer is found, such as these actions occurring in dar al-harb, or a shubha arises in their proof, then these actions will not be valid. This same ruling applies to the coerced person: his statements and actions are valid, but if a modifier is found, then his statements and actions will not be valid and correct, as they will explain later that in some situations the statement and action of the coerced person become invalid and are attributed to the coercer.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a general principle from the author (al-musannif) summarizing that duress does not negate legal eligibility (ahliyyah) nor drop the legal address (khitab) from the coerced person. Therefore, duress alone does not invalidate statements (like divorce or emancipation) or actions (like murder or property destruction). The reasoning is that a coerced person retains ikhtiyar (choice) even if riza (consent) is lost. However, if there is an external modifying factor (mujir) or evidence that changes the ruling, the statement or action may become invalid—just as with a non-coerced person. Examples given: a divorce pronounced with a condition (ta'liq) or exception (istithna') does not take effect; actions like drinking wine or zina are normally punishable, but if they occur in dar al-harb (abode of war) or shubha exists, the punishment is dropped. The same logic applies to the coerced person: his actions are generally valid, but a modifier (such as complete duress that changes attribution) can invalidate them. This principle is foundational in Hanafi usul al-fiqh regarding ikrah.

Key Terms
اکراہ (ikrāh): Compulsion or duress that affects the validity of statements and actions by removing consent but not necessarily choice.
مغیر (mujīr): A modifying factor or evidence that changes the ruling of a statement or action, rendering it invalid.
اہلیت (ahliyyah): Legal capacity to acquire rights and obligations; duress does not negate it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The divorce is valid only if a modifier is absent.
D) The divorce is suspended until duress ends.

Key Takeaway
Duress does not inherently invalidate acts; only an external modifier can change the ruling, preserving accountability while allowing for exceptions.

Effects of Duress: Complete and Incomplete
Explains where the effect of duress appears: complete duress changes attribution; incomplete duress nullifies consent; contracts voidable; iqrar invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between complete and incomplete duress and state their distinct effects on attribution, consent, contracts, marriage dissolution, and confessions.

Core Idea
Complete duress shifts attribution; incomplete duress only removes consent; iqrar is always invalid.

This passage explores the nuanced effects of duress in Hanafi law. It addresses a critical question: if duress does not outright invalidate actions, where does its effect appear? The answer reveals two distinct types of duress — complete and incomplete — each with different consequences for attribution, consent, and the validity of contracts and confessions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of duress
Read the passage and note the distinction between complete duress (ikrah kamil) and incomplete duress (ikrah qasir).
Complete duress involves a threat that overwhelms choice; incomplete duress involves threats that only affect consent.
2
State the effect on attribution
For complete duress, the action is attributed to the coercer, not the coerced person, provided the action can change attribution.
This applies to actions like killing or property destruction, not to statements like talaq.
3
State the effect on consent and choice
Incomplete duress removes consent (rida) but not choice (ikhtiyar). The coerced person still has capacity but lacks willing consent.
Both complete and incomplete duress remove consent, but only complete duress changes attribution.
4
Apply to contracts and personal status actions
Contracts (sale, lease) that depend on consent become voidable; they can be ratified after duress ends. Talaq and itaq do not require consent, so they are effective immediately under duress.
This is a key distinction: voidable vs. effective.
5
Analyze the rule on confessions (iqrar)
Any confession made under duress is invalid because duress indicates a high probability of falsehood, and the truth of the confession cannot be established.
This applies to both complete and incomplete duress and covers all dispositions, whether voidable or not.
