Meaning of Hatta in Conditional Oaths
Analysis of the particle 'hatta' in oath statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the particle 'hatta' in oath statements and determine its legal meaning based on whether the verbs belong to the same person or different persons.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, 'hatta' in oaths can indicate immediate sequence (like 'fa') not just purpose or causation.

Particles in Arabic shift meaning based on context. This lesson explores a specific case: the use of 'hatta' in conditional oaths where both verbs belong to the swearer. The Hanafi analysis shows that 'hatta' here is not for purpose or causation but for conjunction and immediate sequence, which has concrete legal consequences for oath-breaking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath formulation
Start by isolating the conditional oath statement from the passage: 'If I do not come to you until I eat with you, then my slave is free.' Note the two verbs after 'hatta'.
The particle 'hatta' links the two actions.
2
Classify the grammatical relationship
Determine whether the verbs belong to the same person or different persons. In this case, both 'coming' and 'eating' are actions of the swearer.
If verbs belong to different persons, different rules apply.
3
Eliminate impossible interpretations
Apply the Hanafi usul principle: a person's action cannot be a purpose (ghaya) or cause (sabab) for his own action. Thus, 'hatta' here cannot indicate purpose or causation.
Causation requires the cause to be external to the actor.
4
Determine the correct meaning
Since purpose and causation are ruled out, 'hatta' must indicate conjunction (like 'fa') meaning immediate sequence. Both actions must occur in immediate succession.
The similarity between purpose and sequence supports this interpretation.
5
Derive the legal ruling
The oath requires both coming and eating immediately. If the swearer does not come, or comes but does not eat immediately (or delays), the oath is broken.
This affects the legal consequence: if all conditions are met, the slave is freed; otherwise not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مخاطب کا فعل ہے، جس میں حالف کو کوئی اختیار نہیں ہے، اس لئے یہی کیا جائے گا کہ حالف تو تغدی کے لئے آیا تو قسم پوری ہوگئی ، حانث نہ ہوگا ۔ لیکن اگر دونوں فعل حالف کے ہوں جیسے ان لم آتک حتی اتغدى عندک فعبدی حد تو اس صورت میں قسم اتیان اور تغدی دونوں سے متعلق ہوگی، اور اس صورت میں حتی نہ غایت کے لئے ہوگا، نہ لام گئی کے معنی میں سبیت کے لئے ہوگا، بلکہ حتی عطف بحرف الفاء کے لئے ہوگا، غایت کے لئے تو اس لئے نہیں ہوگا کہ کھانا کھلانا اتیان کے لئے غایت نہیں بن سکتا ہے، اور لام گئی کے معنی میں اس لئے نہیں ہوگا کہ اتیان اور تغد یہ دونوں ایک ہی شخص کے فعل ہیں ، عادہ ایک شخص کا فعل خود اس کے فعل کے لئے سبب اور جزا نہیں بن سکتا، لہذا کھانا کھلانا اتیان کا سبب نہ ہوگا، تو حتی لام گئی کے معنی میں سبیت و مجازات کے لئے نہ ہوگا، بلکہ عطف بحرف الفاء کے لئے ہوگا، اور عطف کے لئے لینے میں مناسبت یہ ہے کہ حتی غایت کے لئے آتا ہے، اور غایت مغیا سے وجود مؤخر ہوتی ہے، اور حرف عطف فا تعقیب کے لئے آتا ہے، یعنی فا کا مابعد اس کے ماقبل سے وجود میں مؤخر ہوتا ہے، تو غایت اور تعقیب کے معنی قریب قریب ہیں۔
اب مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ اگر میں تیرے پاس نہ آیا پھر تیرے پاس کھانا نہ کھایا،تو میرا غلام آزاد ہے،تو آنا اور فوراً کھانا کھانا یہ دونوں قسم پورا ہونے کے لئے ضروری ہیں، چنانچہ حالف مخاطب کے پاس آیا پھر متصلاً کھانا بھی کھایا ، تو قسم پوری ہو جائے گی ، اور غلام آزاد نہ ہوگا، اور اگر حالف آیا ہی نہیں، یا آیا مگر کھانا نہیں کھا یا ، یا کچھ تاخیر سے کھانا کھایا ، تو ان تمام صورتوں میں حانث ہو جائے گا اور غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔
It is the action of the addressee, in which the swearer has no choice; therefore it will be judged that if the swearer came for eating, the oath is fulfilled, he is not an oath-breaker. But if both actions are the swearer's, like 'If I do not come to you until I eat with you, then my slave is free,' then in this case the oath relates to both coming and eating. In this case, 'hatta' is not for purpose (ghaya), nor in the sense of 'lam' for causation (sababiyya), but rather 'hatta' is for conjunction like the letter 'fa'. It is not for purpose because eating cannot be the purpose of coming. It is not for causation because both coming and eating are actions of the same person; generally, a person's action cannot be the cause and recompense for his own action. So 'hatta' is not for causation and recompense, but for conjunction like 'fa'. The suitability of interpreting it as conjunction is that 'hatta' is used for purpose, and the purpose is posterior to the intended act, and the conjunctive letter 'fa' is for immediate sequence, meaning what comes after 'fa' is posterior in existence to what precedes it, so the meanings of purpose and immediate sequence are close. Now the meaning will be: If I do not come to you and then do not eat with you, then my slave is free. So coming and immediately eating are both necessary for the oath to be fulfilled. Thus, if the swearer came to the addressee and then immediately ate, the oath is fulfilled and the slave is not free. But if the swearer did not come at all, or came but did not eat, or ate after some delay, then in all these cases he becomes an oath-breaker and the slave is freed.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
The text is a commentary on the use of the Arabic particle 'hatta' in oath formulations. It begins by distinguishing cases where the verb after 'hatta' belongs to the addressee (in which case the oath is fulfilled by the swearer's coming) from cases where both verbs belong to the swearer. In the latter case, 'hatta' cannot indicate purpose (because eating is not a purpose of coming) nor causation (because a person cannot cause his own act). Instead, 'hatta' is interpreted as a conjunctive particle equivalent to 'fa', indicating immediate sequence. Consequently, both actions (coming and eating immediately) are required for the oath to be fulfilled. If the swearer fails to come, or comes but does not eat immediately, he breaks the oath. This analysis is typical of Hanafi usul, which pays careful attention to the literal meanings and logical relationships of particles.

Key Terms
حتى (hatta): In this context, it is interpreted as a conjunctive particle meaning immediate sequence, not purpose or causation.
غاية (ghaya): Purpose or goal; something for which an action is done.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the oath 'If I do not come to you until I eat with you, then my slave is free,' what does 'hatta' mean according to Hanafi usul?
A) It indicates purpose (ghaya).
B) It indicates causation (sababiyya).
C) It indicates conjunction (like fa).
D) It indicates exception.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the meaning of 'hatta' in conditional oaths is determined by grammatical context; when both verbs are by the same person, it indicates immediate sequence, not purpose or causation.

Meanings of Prepositions Ba and 'Ala in Hanafi Usul
Linguistic legal analysis of two prepositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the primary meanings of 'ba' (adhesion) and 'ala' (obligation) in Hanafi usul, and explain their extended uses in conditional oaths and pure compensations, citing a Quranic example.

Core Idea
Ba means adhesion, 'ala means obligation; their affinity allows 'ala to replace 'ba' in pure compensations.

In Hanafi legal theory, prepositions are not mere grammar—they carry binding legal implications. The particle 'ba' indicates adhesion (ilṣāq), affecting how oaths and contracts are interpreted. The particle 'ala' denotes obligation (ilzām) and is metaphorically extended to conditions and pure compensations. This lesson unpacks these meanings using an oath scenario and a Quranic verse, showing how linguistic precision shapes legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary meanings
From the text, determine the core meaning of 'ba' (adhesion) and 'ala' (obligation). Note that 'ala originally meant elevation but is used figuratively for obligation.
This is foundational for understanding the legal applications that follow.
2
Apply 'ba' to an oath scenario
Consider the example: 'If you inform me (bi) the arrival of so-and-so...' Explain why the 'ba' requires truthful news for the oath to be broken. Trace the reasoning that news must adhere to reality.
This shows how adhesion (ilṣāq) operates in legal language.
3
Analyze 'ala' in the Quranic verse
Read the verse 'يُبَايِعْنَكَ عَلَى أَنْ لَا يُشْرِكْنَ' (60:12). Identify why 'ala' here indicates a condition (shart) and how this connects to its primary meaning of obligation.
The connection: a condition imposes an obligation on the party fulfilling it.
4
Understand the substitution of 'ala' for 'ba' in pure compensations
The text states that 'ala' can replace 'ba' in pure compensations (mu'awadat mahda) because obligation (ilzām) entails adhesion (ilṣāq). Work through the reasoning: if something is obligatory, it is effectively attached.
This expansion is limited to pure compensations like sale, lease, and marriage.
5
Summarize the legal implications
Synthesize: How do these meanings affect contract interpretation? For example, in a marriage contract, 'ala' in 'upon a dower' imposes an obligation. Contrast with non-pure compensations where 'ala' cannot replace 'ba'.
This step prepares you for the practice tasks.

Source Text

وَمِنْ ذَلِكَ حُرُوفُ الْجَرَ فَالْبَاءَ لِلْإِلْصَاقِ وَلِهَذَا قُلْنَا فِي قَوْلِهِ إِنْ أَخْبَرْتَنِيْ بِقُدُوْم فَلَانٍ أَنَّهُ يَقَعُ عَلَى الصِّدْقِ وَعَلَى لِلْألْزَامِ فِى قَوْلِهِ عَلَى اَلْفْ وَتَسْتَعْمَلُ لِلشَّرْطِ قَالَ الله تَعَالَى يُبَا يَعْنَكَ عَلَى أَنْ لَا يُشْرِكْنَ بِااللَّهِ شَيْئًا وَتُسْتَعَارُ بِمَعْنَى الْبَاءِ فِي الْمُعَاوَضَاتِ الْمَحْضَةِ لِاَنَّ الْإِلْصَاقَ يُنَاسِبُ اللُّزُوْمَ - ترجمہ: اور حروف معانی میں سے حروف جر ہیں، پس باء الصاق کے لئے ہے، اسی وجہ سے ہم نے کہا قائل کے قول ان اخبرتنى بقدوم فلان “ میں کہ یہ کلام سچی خبر پر واقع ہوگا۔
اور علی الزام کے لئے ہے قائل کے قول ” علی الف میں اور شرط کے لئے بھی استعمال کیا جاتا ہے، اللہ تعالیٰ نے ارشاد فرمایا یبایعنک اعلی ان لا يشركن با الله شيئا اور کلمہ علی مستعار لیا جاتا ہے باء کے معنی میں معاوضات محضہ میں، اس لئے کہ الصاق لزوم کے مناسب ہے۔
شریح:- مصنف حروف عاطفہ کے بعد حروف جر کو بیان کر رہے ہیں،حروف جارہ میں ایک حرف ’باء “ ہے، جو الصاق کے لئے آتا ہے، جیسے مدرت بزید میں مررت ملصق بہ ہے، اور زید ملصق ہے، اس میں الصاق مجازاً ہے اور بہ دائی “ اس میں الصاق حقیقہ ہے۔
مصنف مثال بیان کر رہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے کہا " ان اخبرتنى بقدوم فلان فعبدی حرمخاطب نے فلاں کے آنے کی خبر دی، تو اگر یہ خبر سچی ہے تو قائل حانث ہو جائے گا، ورنہ نہیں، کیونکہ باء الصاق کے لئے ہے ، تو مطلب یہ ہو گا ان اخبرتنى خبرا ملصقا بقدوم فلان ، اگر تو نے مجھے ایسی خبر دی جو ملصق ہو فلاں کے قدوم سے، اور خبر قدوم کے ساتھ اسی وقت ملصق ہوگی جب خبر سچی ہو، لہذا اس کا کلام سچی خبر پر محمول ہو گا۔
وعلى الالزام:- حروف جارہ میں ایک حرف علی ہے، جو الزام کے لئے آتا ہے، جیسے لَهُ عَلَيَّ الْفْ “ اس کے میرے ذمہ ایک ہزار روپئے ہیں یعنی میرے اوپر لازم ہے، علی کی اصل وضع الزام کے لئے نہیں ہے، بلکہ استعلاء کے لیے ہے، پھر استعلاء بھی حقیقہ ہوتا ہے جیسے زید علی السطح زید چھت پر ہے، حقیقت میں بلندی پر ہے، اور کبھی استعلاء حکما ہوتا ہے جیسے له عَلَى الْفْ۔
علی بھی مجاز اشرط کے لئے بھی استعمال ہوتا ہے ، جیسے ارشاد باری تعالیٰ ہے "یبا یعنك على ان لا يشركن با اللہ شیئا وہ عورتیں آپ سے بیعت کریں اس شرط پر کہ اللہ کے ساتھ کسی کو شریک نہ کریں گی حقیقی اور مجازی معنی میں مناسبت یہ ہے کہ علی الزام کے لئے آتا ہے، اور جزاء میں بھی الزام شرط ہوتا ہے، یعنی وجود جزاء کے لئے شرط لازم ہے۔
اور علی کبھی مجاز امعاوضات محضہ میں باء کے معنی میں استعمال ہوتا ہے، معاوضات محضہ اس کو کہتے ہیں جس میں عوض اصلی ہو، کبھی جدا نہ ہو جیسے بیج ، اجارہ ، نکاح وغیرہ ، معاوضات محضہ کہہ کر معاوضات غیر محصہ کو خارج کر دیا جیسے طلاق بالمال، عتاق بالمال وغیرہ۔ بہر حال معاوضہ میں علی باء کے معنی میں مجاز ا استعمال ہوتا ہے جیسے بعث على كذا، نكحت على كذا يعني بکذا، مناسبت یہ ہے کہ باء الصاق کے لئے ہے، اور علی الزام کے لئے ہے اور الصاق والزام میں مناسبت ہے کیونکہ جب ایک چیز دوسری چیز کے لئے لازم ہوگی تو وہ ملصق بھی ہوگی بلزوم کے ساتھ الصاق ضرور ہوگا، لہذ اعلی کو باء کے معنی میں لے سکتے ہیں۔
And among that are the particles of jarr. So 'ba' is for adhesion (ilṣāq). For this reason we said regarding his statement 'If you inform me of the arrival of so-and-so' that it occurs upon truth. And 'ala' is for obligation (ilzām) in his statement 'upon me a thousand'. And it is used for condition. Allah Almighty said: 'They pledge allegiance to you upon [the condition] that they do not associate anything with Allah.' And it is borrowed in the meaning of 'ba' in pure compensations because adhesion suits obligation. Translation: And among the particles of meaning are the particles of jarr. So 'ba' is for adhesion. For this reason we said regarding the speaker's statement 'If you inform me of the arrival of so-and-so' that this speech occurs upon truthful news. And 'ala' is for obligation in the speaker's statement 'upon me a thousand'. And it is also used for condition. Allah Almighty said: 'They pledge allegiance to you upon that they do not associate anything with Allah.' And the word 'ala' is borrowed in the meaning of 'ba' in pure compensations, because adhesion suits obligation. Explanation: The author is explaining the particles of jarr after the particles of 'atf. Among the particles of jarr is the letter 'ba', which is used for adhesion, like 'I passed by Zaid' means 'I passed adhering to him', and Zaid is the one adhered to. This adhesion is metaphorical. In 'I gave a dirham' it is real adhesion. The author gives an example: If someone said, 'If you inform me of the arrival of so-and-so, then my slave is free,' and the addressee gave news of so-and-so's arrival, then if the news is true, the speaker becomes an oath-breaker; otherwise not. Because 'ba' is for adhesion, so the meaning is: 'If you give me news adhering to the arrival of so-and-so.' The news will adhere to the arrival only when the news is true. Therefore, his statement is interpreted as referring to true news. And 'ala' for obligation: Among the particles of jarr is the letter 'ala', which is used for obligation, like 'he has upon me a thousand' meaning it is incumbent upon me. The original meaning of 'ala' is not obligation but elevation. Elevation can be real, like 'Zaid is on the roof', literally in a high place, and sometimes elevation is figurative, like 'he has upon me a thousand'. 'Ala' is also used metaphorically for condition, as in the divine command: 'They pledge allegiance to you upon the condition that they do not associate anything with Allah.' The women pledge to you on this condition. The suitability between the real and metaphorical meanings is that 'ala' is used for obligation, and in a recompense (jaza') there is also obligation of the condition, meaning the existence of the recompense requires the condition. And 'ala' is sometimes used metaphorically in the meaning of 'ba' in pure compensations (mu'awadat mahda). Pure compensations are those in which the exchange is primary and never separate, like sale, lease, marriage, etc. By saying 'pure compensations', non-pure compensations are excluded, like divorce for money, manumission for money, etc. In any case, in a compensation, 'ala' is used metaphorically in the meaning of 'ba', such as 'I sold upon such', 'I married upon such', meaning 'with such'. The suitability is that 'ba' is for adhesion and 'ala' is for obligation, and there is suitability between adhesion and obligation because when one thing is obligatory for another, it will also be adhering; with obligation, adhesion necessarily occurs. Therefore, 'ala' can be taken in the meaning of 'ba'.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
The text explains the primary and metaphorical uses of two Arabic prepositions: 'ba' and 'ala'. According to Hanafi usul, 'ba' fundamentally indicates adhesion (ilṣāq). In the context of an oath, if someone says 'If you inform me of the arrival of so-and-so', the 'ba' here causes the statement to be interpreted as requiring truthful news, because the news must adhere to the reality of the arrival. Therefore, if the news is false, the oath is not broken. 'Ala' primarily indicates obligation (ilzām) but originally means elevation. It is used in debts and contracts to denote an obligation upon someone. It is also used metaphorically for conditions, as in the Quranic verse about the pledge of women. Furthermore, 'ala' can be used in place of 'ba' in pure compensations (like sales and marriages) because adhesion and obligation are closely related: something that is obligatory also adheres. This analysis is typical of Hanafi linguistic reasoning, where the literal meanings of particles are expanded to cover contractual contexts.

Key Terms
باء (ba): A preposition indicating adhesion (ilṣāq); used to show attachment of one thing to another.
على (ala): A preposition indicating obligation (ilzām); originally means elevation, used metaphorically for conditions and in place of 'ba' in pure compensations.
معاوضات محضة (mu'awadat mahda): Pure compensations: contracts where exchange is primary and inseparable, like sale, lease, marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary meaning of the preposition 'ba'?
A) Adhesion (ilṣāq).
B) Obligation (ilzām).
C) Condition (shart).
D) Elevation (isti'lā').

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the literal meanings of 'ba' (adhesion) and 'ala' (obligation) guide the interpretation of oaths and contracts, with metaphorical extensions permitted when justified by semantic affinity.
